The Grammar and Theology of 'Subhana man taqaddasat'
Grammatical and theological analysis of the opening phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical structure of the phrase 'Subhana man taqaddasat' and its theological implications for divine transcendence and attributes.

Core Idea
'Subhan' is not just praise—it is a verbal noun constructed to indicate perpetual distancing from evil.

This passage delves into the opening words of the original text, 'Subhana man taqaddasat'. The commentator provides a detailed grammatical analysis, showing that 'Subhan' is a verbal noun (masdar) used to express perpetual glorification, and then explains the meaning of 'taqaddus' and related terms like 'subahat' and 'jamal' in their theological context. Understanding these roots is essential for grasping how classical scholars articulated God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the grammatical terms and alternative interpretations. Then read the translation to get the full meaning.
Pay attention to the structure: the author first discusses 'Subhan', then 'taqaddus', then 'subahat' and 'jamal'.
2
Understand why the verb is omitted
The commentator states that the verb in 'Subhana' is omitted to indicate perpetual and constant glorification. This is a key grammatical point that shapes the meaning.
This omission is intentional and not just for brevity.
3
Examine the alternative meanings of 'Subhan'
The passage lists several possible derivations and meanings for 'Subhan', including distancing in land/water and quickness in obedience. Note which ones are rejected and why.
The derivation from saying 'Subhan Allah' is rejected because it would cause circularity.
4
Explore the meaning of 'taqaddus' and related terms
'Taqaddus' means purification, and the tafa''ul form indicates intensification. Also, note the explanation of 'subahat' as lights and 'jamal' as beauty, and their connection to divine attributes.
The annexation of 'subahat' to God is either possessive or specifying.
5
Connect to theological concepts
Reflect on how these linguistic analyses support the theological understanding of God's transcendence and affirmative attributes.
The terms 'subahat' and 'jamal' are linked to the affirmative attributes (al-sifaat al-thubutiyya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (سبحان من تقدست) نصب على المصدر بمعنى التنزيه والتبعيد من السوء، أي: أسبح سبحاناً حذف الفعل لقصد الدوام والثبات صرّح به الشيخ الرضي، وأقيم المصدر مقامه، وأضيف إلى المفعول وحذفه واجب قياسا فهو مصدر من المجرد يستعمل بمعنى المزيد كما في أنبت الله نباتاً، ويجوز أن يكون مصدر سبح في الأرض والماء إذا ذهب فيهما وأبعد أي أبعد من السوء إبعاداً ، أو من إدراك العقول وإحاطته وقيل : معناه السرعة والخفة في الطاعة، ولا يجوز أن يكون من سبح كمنع أو سبح تسبيحاً بمعنى قال: سبحان الله للزوم الدور. والتقدس التطهر من قدس في الأرض إذا ذهب لأن المتطهر عن الشيء متبعد عنه، والتفعل للمبالغة والسبحات بضم السين والباء : الأنوار جمع سبحة والجمال الحسن في الخلق والخلق جمل ككرم فهو جميل كأمير وغراب وزمان. وفي الاصطلاح الصفات الثبوتية وإضافة السبحات إليه إما لامية أو إضافة
His saying: 'Subhana man taqaddasat' (Glory be to Him who is sanctified) — it is in the accusative as a verbal noun (maṣdar) meaning disavowal and distancing from evil, i.e., 'I glorify with a glorification' (usabbiḥu subḥānan). The verb has been omitted for the purpose of perpetual and constant [glorification], as declared by al-Shaykh al-Raḍī, and the verbal noun is put in its place, and it is annexed to the object, and its omission is obligatory by analogy. So it is a verbal noun from the simple verb used in the sense of the augmented verb, as in 'anbata Allahu nabātan' (Allah caused to grow a growth). And it is permissible that it be a verbal noun of 'sabaḥa' in the land and water, meaning he went forth and distanced himself, i.e., he distanced himself from evil with distancing; or from the comprehension of intellects and their encompassment. And it is said: its meaning is quickness and lightness in obedience. And it is not permissible that it be from 'sabaḥa' like 'manaʿa', or from 'sabbaḥa tashbīḥan' meaning he said 'subḥāna Allah' because of the necessary circularity. And 'al-taqaddus' is purification; from 'qadusa' in the land, meaning he went, because the one purified from something is far from it; and the tafaʿʿul form indicates intensification. And 'al-subaḥāt' with ḍamma on the sīn and bā': the lights, plural of 'subḥa'; and 'al-jamāl' is beauty in character and form; 'jamala' like 'karuma', so he is 'jamīl' like 'amīr', 'ghurāb', and 'zamān'. And in terminology, it is the affirmative attributes (al-ṣifāt al-thubūtiyya); and the annexation of 'al-subaḥāt' to Him is either for possession/latitude or for specification (lāmīyya or iḍāfa).
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed philological commentary on the opening phrase of the original text: 'سبحان من تقدست' (Glory be to Him who is sanctified). The commentator explains the grammatical status of 'سبحان' as an accusative verbal noun (maṣdar) meaning disavowal and distancing from evil. He notes that the verb is omitted to indicate perpetual glorification, following al-Shaykh al-Raḍī. The verbal noun stands in place of the verb and is annexed to the object, with the object necessarily omitted by analogy. It is a maṣdar from a simple verb (mujarrad) used in the sense of an augmented verb (mazīd), similar to 'anbata Allahu nabātan'. Alternative interpretations are given: it could be from 'sabaḥa' meaning to go forth in land and water, implying distancing from evil; or from the intellect's inability to encompass. Some say it means quickness and lightness in obedience. The commentator rules out deriving it from 'sabaḥa' (like manaʿa) or from 'sabbaḥa tashbīḥan' because that would lead to circularity. Then he turns to 'al-taqaddus', explaining it as purification, from 'qadusa' meaning to go, with the tafaʿʿul form indicating intensification. 'Al-subaḥāt' (the lights) is the plural of 'subḥa'; 'al-jamāl' is beauty in character and form. In theological terminology, 'ṣifāt thubūtiyya' are the affirmative attributes of God. The annexation of 'al-subaḥāt' to Him is either possessive (lāmīyya) or specifying (iḍāfa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Subhan' simply means 'glory' or 'praise' without further nuance.
Correction: The commentator emphasizes that 'Subhan' specifically means disavowal and distancing from evil, and is constructed as a verbal noun for perpetual glorification, not just a declaration of praise.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the verb omitted in the construction of 'سبحان'?
A) To avoid repetition.
B) To indicate perpetual and constant glorification.
C) Because the verb is understood from context.
D) To make the sentence shorter.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'Subhana man taqaddasat' is carefully constructed to affirm God's perpetual transcendence and distance from any imperfection, using a verbal noun that implies continuous action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Subhan Allah', do you consciously intend to distance God from every imperfection in your mind, or has the phrase become a mere habit? What would it take for you to make each utterance a deliberate act of tanzih?
?
The passage explains that omitting the verb from 'Subhan' indicates perpetual glorification. Does your glorification of Allah feel like a constant state, or is it confined to specific times? What practical step can you take today to cultivate a continuous awareness of His transcendence?
?
The author rejects certain derivations because they lead to circular reasoning. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that rely on circular logic (e.g., defining Him by what you've been told without deeper reflection)? How can you break that cycle and ground your understanding in clear evidence?

Positive Attributes and Key Terms
Author defines positive attributes and glosses key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of positive attributes (al-sifaat al-thubutiyya) and the meaning of key terms like huduth, zawal, and tanazzuh.

Core Idea
Positive attributes are like lights manifesting God's beauty.

The author begins by defining the positive attributes as those that are like lights in manifestation and beauty. He then glosses several terms that appear in the doxology, explaining their lexical meanings. Understanding these terms helps us grasp the richness of the text's description of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to get an overview of the terms being defined.
2
Identify the definition of positive attributes
Notice how the author describes them as lights in manifestation and beauty. This sets the tone for understanding God's attributes as luminous perfections.
3
Learn the lexical glosses
Go through each term: simah (mark), huduth (origination), zawal (passing away), tanazzuh (transcendence), saradiqat (tents). These are important for interpreting the doxology.
Some terms are related to God (tanazzuh) and others to creation (huduth, zawal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في عقائد المشبه به إلى المشبه أي الصفات الثبوتية التي هي كالأنوار في الظهور والبهاء، والسمة أثر الكي وسمه يسمه وسماً ،وسمة والحدوث الوجود بعد العدم والزوال العدم بعد الوجود، والتنزه التباعد والسرادقات جمع سرادق : وهو الذي يمد فوق صحن الدار يقال له : سرا برده، والجلال مصدر جل الشيء عظم، وفي الاصطلاح الصفات السلبية لأنها موجبة لعظمة ذاته تعالى، وتعاليه عن المماثلة والإدراك والإضافة كما في سبحات جماله والمناسبة بين السرادقات والصفات السلبية أن كل واحدة منهما موجبة للاحتجاب وعطف تنزهت على تقدست للاتحاد في المعنى والاختلاف في المتعلق تلالات أي لمعت وصفحة كل شيء جانبه وصفحات الموجودات عوارضها من الوجود وما يتبعه من الكمالات والجبروت فعلوت للمبالغة من الجبر بمعنى القهر والسلطنة وفي الاصطلاح الصفات الفعلية أي لمعت على عوارض الموجودات آثار صفاته الفعلية من الإيجاد والإعدام والتغيير من حال إلى حال وفيه إشارة إلى أن الماهيات غير مجعولة ولم يعطف هذه الصلة على ما قبله للإشارة إلى استقلاله في استيجاب التسبيح دفعاً لتوهم النقص والسوء فيها من تعلقها بالشرور والتهلل التلألؤ والوجنة ما ارتفع من الخدين وفيه أربع لغات : وجنة ووجنة واجنة واجنة والملكوت كرهبوت وترقوت العز والسلطان والمملكة وهذه الفقرة متحدة بما قبلها في المآل مغايرة له باعتبار التعبير وزيادة الإحسان فإن آثار صفاته الفعلية من حيث إنها موجبة للتغير مظهر لعزته وسلطنته ومن حيث إنها نعم موجبة لكمال الموجودات
In the beliefs of that which is compared to that to which comparison is made, i.e., the positive attributes (al-sifaat al-thubutiyya) which are like lights in manifestation and beauty. And al-simah is the mark of branding, and he branded it, branding. And al-huduth is existence after non-existence, and al-zawal is non-existence after existence, and al-tanazzuh is distancing. And al-saradiqat is plural of surādiq: that which is spread over the courtyard of the house, called a tent. And al-jalal is the source of 'the thing is great', and in terminology it refers to the negative attributes (al-sifaat al-salbiyya) because they necessitate the greatness of His essence, exalted, and His transcendence above likeness, perception, and relation, as in the beams of His beauty. The suitability between the tents and the negative attributes is that each of them necessitates concealment. And the conjunction of 'tanazzahat' upon 'taqaddasat' is for unity in meaning and difference in the object. Talā'āt means it shone, and safha of everything is its side. And safahat al-mawjudat are their accidents from existence and what follows it of perfections. And al-jabarut is an exaggeration form from al-jabr meaning compulsion and sovereignty, and in terminology it refers to the active attributes (al-sifaat al-fi'liyya), i.e., the effects of His active attributes shone upon the accidents of existents, from bringing into existence and annihilating and changing from one state to another. In it is an indication that essences (al-mahiyyat) are not made. And he did not conjoin this clause to what preceded it to indicate its independence in necessitating praise, to repulse the illusion of deficiency and evil in them due to their connection with evils. And al-tahallul is glitter, and al-wajna is the elevated part of the cheek; it has four dialects: wajna, wujna, ajna, and ijna. And al-malakut, like rahabut and tarqut, is might, sovereignty, and kingdom. And this paragraph is unified with what preceded it in outcome, differing in expression and increase of beneficence. For the effects of His active attributes, insofar as they necessitate change, are a manifestation of His might and sovereignty, and insofar as they are blessings, are necessitating the perfection of existents.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and terminological commentary on phrases from the opening doxology. The author explains key terms used in describing divine attributes. He begins by glossing 'al-mushabbah bihi ila al-mushabbah' as the positive attributes (al-sifaat al-thubutiyya), which are like lights in manifestation. Then he defines several words: al-simah (mark), al-huduth (origination after non-existence), al-zawal (passing away), al-tanazzuh (transcendence), al-saradiqat (tents), and al-jalal (majesty). He states that in kalam terminology, al-jalal refers to the negative attributes (al-sifaat al-salbiyya) because they indicate God's greatness and transcendence. He explains the suitability between tents and negative attributes: both cause concealment. He notes the stylistic conjunction of 'tanazzahat' (transcended) with 'taqaddasat' (sanctified) for rhetorical unity. Then he defines talā'āt (it shone), safha (side), and explains that al-jabarut (omnipotence) in terminology denotes the active attributes (al-sifaat al-fi'liyya), i.e., the effects of God's acts on existents. He mentions that this implies the non-createdness of essences (al-mahiyyat) and why the clause is not conjoined to avoid implying deficiency. Finally, he defines al-tahallul (glitter), al-wajna (cheek), and al-malakut (dominion), and summarizes that the paragraph is unified with the previous in meaning but distinct in expression, highlighting that God's active attributes manifest His might and beneficence.

Key Terms
الصفات الثبوتية
الحدوث
الزوال
التنزه
السرادقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does al-huduth mean?
A) Existence after non-existence
B) Non-existence after existence
C) A mark of branding
D) A tent

Key Takeaway
Positive attributes are like lights, and terms like huduth and zawal describe temporal existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How do you view God's attributes? Are they like lights that manifest His beauty to you, or do you see them as abstract concepts? What would change if you truly believed that His attributes are as clear and beautiful as lights?
?
The term 'al-tanazzuh' means distancing. In what areas of your life do you need to distance yourself from worldly attachments to truly embody tanzih (transcendence)?
?
If huduth (origination) reminds you that everything other than God came into existence after non-existence, how does that affect your attachment to created things? Are you holding onto something that will inevitably pass away (zawal)?

Negative Attributes and the Tents Analogy
Author explains negative attributes and the tents analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term al-jalal denotes negative attributes (al-sifaat al-salbiyya) and why the analogy of tents is appropriate.

Core Idea
Negative attributes emphasize God's transcendence and concealment.

The author moves from positive attributes to negative attributes, explaining that the word 'jalal' (majesty) is used in kalam to refer to attributes that negate imperfections. He draws a striking analogy between these attributes and tents, both of which cause concealment. This lesson unpacks that analogy and the rhetorical nuance in the doxology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on negative attributes
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the definition of jalal and the tents analogy.
2
Understand the terminology
Note that 'jalal' in kalam means negative attributes, and these attributes necessitate God's greatness and transcendence above likeness, perception, and relation.
3
Analyze the tents analogy
Consider why tents and negative attributes both necessitate concealment. This shows how God's essence is hidden from direct understanding.
4
Observe the stylistic conjunction
Notice that 'tanazzahat' is conjuncted to 'taqaddasat' for unity in meaning though different objects. This is a rhetorical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في عقائد المشبه به إلى المشبه أي الصفات الثبوتية التي هي كالأنوار في الظهور والبهاء، والسمة أثر الكي وسمه يسمه وسماً ،وسمة والحدوث الوجود بعد العدم والزوال العدم بعد الوجود، والتنزه التباعد والسرادقات جمع سرادق : وهو الذي يمد فوق صحن الدار يقال له : سرا برده، والجلال مصدر جل الشيء عظم، وفي الاصطلاح الصفات السلبية لأنها موجبة لعظمة ذاته تعالى، وتعاليه عن المماثلة والإدراك والإضافة كما في سبحات جماله والمناسبة بين السرادقات والصفات السلبية أن كل واحدة منهما موجبة للاحتجاب وعطف تنزهت على تقدست للاتحاد في المعنى والاختلاف في المتعلق تلالات أي لمعت وصفحة كل شيء جانبه وصفحات الموجودات عوارضها من الوجود وما يتبعه من الكمالات والجبروت فعلوت للمبالغة من الجبر بمعنى القهر والسلطنة وفي الاصطلاح الصفات الفعلية أي لمعت على عوارض الموجودات آثار صفاته الفعلية من الإيجاد والإعدام والتغيير من حال إلى حال وفيه إشارة إلى أن الماهيات غير مجعولة ولم يعطف هذه الصلة على ما قبله للإشارة إلى استقلاله في استيجاب التسبيح دفعاً لتوهم النقص والسوء فيها من تعلقها بالشرور والتهلل التلألؤ والوجنة ما ارتفع من الخدين وفيه أربع لغات : وجنة ووجنة واجنة واجنة والملكوت كرهبوت وترقوت العز والسلطان والمملكة وهذه الفقرة متحدة بما قبلها في المآل مغايرة له باعتبار التعبير وزيادة الإحسان فإن آثار صفاته الفعلية من حيث إنها موجبة للتغير مظهر لعزته وسلطنته ومن حيث إنها نعم موجبة لكمال الموجودات

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and terminological commentary on phrases from the opening doxology. The author explains key terms used in describing divine attributes. He begins by glossing 'al-mushabbah bihi ila al-mushabbah' as the positive attributes (al-sifaat al-thubutiyya), which are like lights in manifestation. Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
What does the analogy of tents illustrate about negative attributes?
A) They are like physical coverings
B) They necessitate concealment

Key Takeaway
Negative attributes (jalal) emphasize God's transcendence and are likened to tents that conceal His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you profess that God is beyond likeness and perception, does this translate into a deep reverence in your heart, or is it just a verbal affirmation? How will you cultivate genuine awe of His concealment today?
?
The tents analogy reminds us that God's greatness is veiled from direct sight. How often do you try to penetrate that veil through intellectual pride, forgetting that the veil itself is a sign of His majesty? What would it look like for you to humbly accept the veil and seek Him through His signs?
?
If you truly believed that God's essence is concealed but His attributes reveal His majesty, how would your worship change? This week, commit to a specific act of worship that acknowledges His transcendence, such as prolonged silence or contemplation of His names.

Active Attributes and Divine Effects
Author defines active attributes and related terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term al-jabarut refers to active attributes (al-sifaat al-fi'liyya) and how these attributes manifest in creation.

Core Idea
Active attributes are God's effects in creation, showing His power and beneficence.

The author concludes by defining active attributes (al-sifaat al-fi'liyya) as those related to God's actions in the world. He explains that the term al-jabarut (omnipotence) is used for these attributes, and they shine upon the accidents of existents. This lesson also addresses a subtle point about the uncreatedness of essences and why the author did not conjoin this clause to the previous one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on active attributes
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the definition of jabarut and the effects of active attributes.
2
Understand al-jabarut
Note that jabarut is an exaggerated form of jabr (compulsion) and in terminology refers to active attributes that bring about existence and non-existence.
3
Grasp the implication about essences
The passage hints that essences (al-mahiyyat) are not made, meaning they are not created in the sense of having no prior reality.
4

The author did not conjoin this clause to avoid implying deficiency due to association with evils.
5

Malakut (dominion) is also related to active attributes, showing that these attributes manifest His sovereignty and perfection.

In the beliefs of that which is compared to that to which comparison is made, i.e., the positive attributes (al-sifaat al-thubutiyya) which are like lights in manifestation and beauty. And al-simah is the mark of branding, and he branded it, branding. And al-huduth is existence after non-existence, and al-zawal is non-existence after existence, and al-tanazzuh is distancing. And al-saradiqat is plural of surādiq: that which is spread over the courtyard of the house, called a tent. And al-jalal is the source of 'the thing is great', and in terminology it refers to the negative attributes (al-sifaat al-salbiyya) because they necessitate the greatness of His essence, exalted, and His transcendence above likeness, perception, and relation, as in the beams of His beauty. The suitability between the tents and the negative attributes is that each of them necessitates concealment. And the conjunction of 'tanazzahat' upon 'taqaddasat' is for unity in meaning and difference in the object. Talā'āt means it shone, and safha of everything is its side. And safahat al-mawjudat are their accidents from existence and what follows it of perfections. And al-jabarut is an exaggeration form from al-jabr meaning compulsion and sovereignty, and in terminology it refers to the active attributes (al-sifaat al-fi'liyya), i.e., the effects of His active attributes shone upon the accidents of existents, from bringing into existence and annihilating and changing from one state to another. In it is an indication that essences (al-mahiyyat) are not made. And he did not conjoin this clause to what preceded it to indicate its independence in necessitating praise, to repulse the illusion of deficiency and evil in them due to their connection with evils. And al-tahallul is glitter, and al-wajna is the elevated part of the cheek; it has four dialects: wajna, wujna, ajna, and ijna. And al-malakut, like rahabut and tarqut, is might, sovereignty, and kingdom. And this paragraph is unified with what preceded it in outcome, differing in expression and increase of beneficence. For the effects of His active attributes, insofar as they necessitate change, are a manifestation of His might and sovereignty, and insofar as they are blessings, are necessitating the perfection of existents.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and terminological commentary on phrases from the opening doxology. The author explains key terms used in describing divine attributes. He begins by glossing 'al-mushabbah bihi ila al-mushabbah' as the positive attributes (al-sifaat al-thubutiyya), which are like lights in manifestation. Then he defines several words: al-simah (mark), al-huduth (origination after non-existence), al-zawal (passing away), al-tanazzuh (transcendence), al-saradiqat (tents), and al-jalal (majesty). He states that in kalam terminology, al-jalal refers to the negative attributes (al-sifaat al-salbiyya) because they indicate God's greatness and transcendence. He explains the suitability between tents and negative attributes: both cause concealment. He notes the stylistic conjunction of 'tanazzahat' (transcended) with 'taqaddasat' (sanctified) for rhetorical unity. Then he defines talā'āt (it shone), safha (side), and explains that al-jabarut (omnipotence) in terminology denotes the active attributes (al-sifaat al-fi'liyya), i.e., the effects of God's acts on existents. He mentions that this implies the non-createdness of essences (al-mahiyyat) and why the clause is not conjoined to avoid implying deficiency. Finally, he defines al-tahallul (glitter), al-wajna (cheek), and al-malakut (dominion), and summarizes that the paragraph is unified with the previous in meaning but distinct in expression, highlighting that God's active attributes manifest His might and beneficence.

Defending the Original Meaning of 'Thumma' in the Command to Reflect
Defense of 'thumma' implying delay despite objections about obligation timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the original meaning of 'thumma' (then) implying delay is defended against the objection that it would postpone the command to reflect beyond the attainment of all intellectual ranks.

Core Idea
The delay of the command is only from the qualification for higher ranks, not from their actual attainment.

This passage presents a key logical objection in kalam methodology: if 'thumma' means temporal delay, does it contradict the timing of religious obligation? The commentator defends the original meaning by carefully distinguishing between different degrees of intellectual perfection and the moment of obligation. Let's follow the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The statement 'And the word thumma upon its original meaning' is followed by an objection that it would necessitate the delay of the command to reflect after the attainment of the four ranks. This is the problem that needs solving.
The four ranks refer to levels of intellectual perfection.
2
Identify the first answer
The response argues that the necessary delay is not from the actual attainment of all four ranks, but only from the first two ranks and from the qualification for the last two. Focus on the distinction between attainment and qualification.
3
Consider the second answer
Even if the delay were from actual attainment, there is no problem because the Ashʿarī position holds that the intelligent child is not obligated until maturity. This shows that the objection is not fatal.
This answer is given as an alternative ('may be answered').
4
Reflect on the method
Notice how the commentator uses precise theological distinctions to preserve a linguistic interpretation. This is a common method in kalam: reconciling textual meanings with doctrinal commitments.
The passage demonstrates that a literal reading of 'thumma' can be harmonized with obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وكلمة ثم على معناها الأصلي قيل عليه : يلزم من ذلك تأخر الأمر بالتفكر عن حصول المراتب الأربع، وليس كذلك ورد بأن اللازم تأخره عن حصول المرتبتين الأوليين، وعن التأهيل للمرتبتين الأخريين لا عن حصولهما بالفعل ولا محذور فيه وقد يجاب بأن لا محذور في الأول أيضاً على تقدير تسليم اللزوم، إذ المذهب الحق عند أهل السنة أن الصبي العاقل ليس
His statement: (And the word 'thumma' upon its original meaning) It was said: That would necessitate the delay of the command to reflect after the attainment of the four ranks, and it is not so. It was responded that the necessary consequence is its delay after the attainment of the first two ranks, and after the qualification for the last two ranks, not after their actual attainment, and there is no problem in that. And it may be answered that there is no problem in the first either upon the assumption of the necessitation, for the correct doctrine according to Ahl al-Sunna is that the intelligent child is not ...
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the interpretation of 'thumma' (then) in the verse. The objection states that if 'thumma' implies temporal delay, then the command to reflect would occur after the attainment of all four ranks of intellectual perfection, which seems problematic because obligation starts earlier. The answer distinguishes between the first two ranks (which are preparatory) and the last two (which are higher). The delay is not from the actual attainment of the last two ranks but from the qualification (ta'hīl) for them. Alternatively, even if the delay were from the actual attainment, it is not problematic because the correct Ashʿarī position is that the intelligent child is not obligated until maturity. The argument thus preserves the original meaning of 'thumma' while resolving the apparent conflict with the timing of obligation.

Correction: The passage shows that the delay concerns higher ranks and qualification, not the basic fact of obligation.

Checkpoint

A) The actual attainment of the last two ranks.
B) The command to reflect itself.
C) The qualification for the last two ranks, not their actual attainment.

Key Takeaway
The original meaning of 'thumma' is preserved by understanding that the command to reflect is delayed only in relation to the qualification for higher intellectual ranks, not from their actual attainment.

The Linguistic Derivation of 'Azali' and Its Pairing with 'Abadi'
Explanation of the derivation of 'azali' and its pairing with 'abadi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic origins of the term 'azali' and why it is paired with 'abadi' to avoid confusion.

Core Idea
The word 'azali' is coined from 'lam yazal' and paired with 'abadi' to denote true pre-eternity.

In this passage, the author delves into the etymology of 'azali', a key theological term. He cites lexical authorities and proposes two possible derivations: one from the phrase 'lam yazal' (has not ceased) and another from the root 'azl' meaning narrowness. He then explains why 'azali' is often accompanied by 'abadi': to distinguish true pre-eternity from mere antiquity, as the word 'qadim' can be ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get a sense of the content.
2
Identify the two proposed etymologies
Notice that the author first cites al-Sihah for a definition, then presents the derivation from 'lam yazal', and later mentions an alternative root 'azl'.
The parallel with 'azani' (spear of Dhi Yazan) illustrates the phonetic change.
3
Understand the alternative etymology
The author also mentions that 'azal' may come from 'azl' meaning narrowness, because the mind is constricted when trying to imagine a beginning.
Similarly, 'abad' is linked to distance because the mind recoils from an end.
4
Examine the pairing with 'abadi'
Read the final part where the author explains that 'azali' is mentioned alongside 'abadi' to prevent misunderstanding, since 'qadim' can have another meaning.
The Quranic example of 'the old palm branch' shows that 'qadim' can mean merely old.
5
Reflect on the nuance
Consider why the author goes through this linguistic detail: to establish precise terminology for theological discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أزلي) ذُكر في الصحاح أن الأزل بالتحريك القدم يقال : هو أزلي ثم قال : ذكر بعض أهل العلم أن أصل هذه الكلمة قولهم للقديم لم يزل، ثم نسب إلى هذا فلم يستقم إلا بالاختصار فقالوا: يزلي ثم أبدلت الياء ألفاً لأنها أخف فقالوا : أزلي، كما يقال في الرمح المنسوب إلى ذي يزن : أزني وقيل : الأزل اسم لما يضيق القلب عن تقدير بدايته من الأزل، وهو الضيق والأبد اسم لما ينفر القلب عن تقدير نهايته من الأبود والبعود. قوله: (ليقارنه لفظ أبدي فإن للقديم معنى آخر كما في قوله تعالى : كالعرجون القديم ﴾ [يسن : ٣٩] ذكر الازلي قرينة للمراد ودفعاً لتوهم البعيد
His statement: ('Azali). It is mentioned in al-Sihah that al-azal (with the vowel point of fatha) means pre-eternity. It is said: he is azali. Then he said: Some scholars mentioned that the origin of this word is their saying for the pre-eternal: 'lam yazal' (has not ceased). Then it was attributed to this, but it was not sound except through abbreviation, so they said: yazali. Then the ya' was changed into an alif because it is lighter, so they said: azali, just as it is said for the spear attributed to Dhi Yazan: azani. And it is said: al-azal is a name for what constricts the heart from imagining its beginning, from al-azl meaning narrowness; and al-abad is a name for what repels the heart from imagining its end, from al-ubud and al-bu'd. His statement: (so that the word abadi accompanies it, for 'pre-eternal' has another meaning as in His statement: 'like the old palm branch' [Ya Sin: 39]. He mentioned azali as a contextual clue for the intended meaning and to prevent misunderstanding the remote [interpretation]).
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and terminological explanation of the word 'azali' (pre-eternal) and its pairing with 'abadi' (post-eternal). The author begins by citing al-Sihah (a renowned Arabic dictionary) which defines 'al-azal' as pre-eternity. He then reports a scholarly opinion about the word's origin: it derives from the phrase 'lam yazal' (has not ceased), which describes the pre-eternal. To form a relative adjective, the Arabs abbreviated it to 'yazali', then changed the ya to alif for ease, yielding 'azali'. A parallel is given from 'azani' (spear from Dhi Yazan). An alternative etymology connects 'azal' to narrowness (al-azl) because the mind cannot conceive a beginning, and 'abad' to distance (al-bu'd) because the mind recoils from conceiving an end. Finally, the author explains why 'azali' is mentioned alongside 'abadi': the word 'qadim' (ancient) can have another meaning (like in the Quranic verse about an old palm branch), so pairing 'azali' with 'abadi' removes ambiguity and prevents the misunderstanding that 'qadim' here means something else.

لفظ أبدي

Correction: The passage shows that 'qadim' can mean 'old' (like an old palm branch), while 'azali' specifically means pre-eternal. Pairing with 'abadi' removes ambiguity.

D) From the word 'qadim' meaning ancient

Key Takeaway

Signs and Miracles: Two Adjectives, One Essence
Commentary explains the grammatical conjunction of 'ayat' and 'mu'jizat' as coordinate adjectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the terms 'ayat' (signs) and 'mu'jizat' (miracles) can be conjoined in Arabic grammar as coordinate adjectives describing the same essence, and why this is theologically significant.

Core Idea
Signs and miracles are not separate categories—they are different descriptions of the same divine act.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif delves into a seemingly grammatical point: why are 'ayat' and 'mu'jizat' linked together in the text? The commentator explains that this conjunction is not a mere listing of two separate things, but a sophisticated way of highlighting that the same divine act can be described from different perspectives. Understanding this requires a grasp of how attributes relate to essence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it starts with 'His saying: 'And also a sign...'
2
Identify the key grammatical structure
The passage explains that 'ayat' is conjoined to 'mu'jizat' as an adjective to an adjective (عطف الصفة على الصفة). Understand that this is based on the idea that the same essence (ذات) when described with different attributes can be considered conceptually different.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why the commentator makes this point. It shows that signs and miracles are not two distinct types of events but rather one reality viewed in two ways—highlighting the unity of divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وآية أيضاً لكونه علامةً دالةً إلخ) وعلى هذا يكون عطف الآيات على المعجزات من قبيل عطف الصفة على الصفة بناءً على أن الذات من حيث اتصافها بهذه الصفة غيرها من حيث اتصافها بتلك، فيحصل التغاير المصحح للعطف وهذا معنى ما يقال : نزل تغاير الصفات منزلة تغاير الذات .
His saying: 'And also a sign because it is an indicative mark etc.' And on this, the conjunction of 'ayat' upon 'mu'jizat' is of the type of conjoining an adjective to an adjective, based on the fact that the essence (dhāt) in terms of being described with this attribute is different from it in terms of being described with that attribute, so there occurs the difference (taghāyur) that justifies the conjunction. And this is the meaning of what is said: 'The difference of attributes takes the place of the difference of essences.'
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological explanation of a phrase from the original text that said 'and also a sign (ayah) because it is an indicative mark...' The commentator explains that the conjunction of 'ayat' (signs) with 'mu'jizat' (miracles) is not a simple listing of two separate categories but rather a conjunction of two adjectives describing the same reality. The justification is that the same essence (dhāt) when described with one attribute is conceptually different from itself when described with another attribute. Thus, a real difference (taghāyur) is achieved which allows the grammatical conjunction (عطف) of the two terms. This principle is summarized by saying that the difference of attributes takes the place of the difference of essences.

Key Terms
آية
معجزة
عطف
صفة
ذات
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'ayat' and 'mu'jizat' refer to two completely separate types of miracles or signs.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they are two descriptions of the same divine act, justified by the conceptual difference arising from different attributes.

Checkpoint

A) Because they refer to two different entities
B) Because the essence is the same but attributes differ
C) Because one is a subset of the other
D) Because they are synonyms

Key Takeaway
The conjunction of 'ayat' and 'mu'jizat' teaches that divine acts can be seen under different attributes while remaining a single reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How would your gratitude change if you saw every sign and every miracle as flowing from the same single, all-powerful Essence?
?
If the difference of attributes does not split the essence, then God's mercy and God's justice are not two parts of God but two ways of describing the same perfect Being. In your heart, do you ever treat God's mercy as a different 'being' from His justice, as if they could conflict? What would it mean to truly believe they are one?

This lesson teaches that the same reality can be described with different adjectives without contradiction. Think of your own life: are there attributes of God (like His love and His anger) that you find difficult to hold together? Are you willing to let go of the mental division and accept that they are both true descriptions of the same Essence? If so, how will that change your fear and hope in prayer?

The Distinction Between Ta'dhim and Tamjid
Distinction between ta'dhim and tamjid based on root meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise terminological distinction between ta'dhim (magnification) and tamjid (glorification) based on their Arabic roots and how they apply to Allah's perfections.

Core Idea
Tamjid is for active perfections; ta'dhim is for essential perfections.

This passage clarifies a subtle but important distinction in Islamic theology between two terms that are often used interchangeably: ta'dhim (magnification) and tamjid (glorification). The commentator traces the root of tamjid to 'majd' (generosity), which implies action and effect, while ta'dhim is reserved for essential or inherent perfections. Understanding this distinction helps us accurately describe Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting how tamjid and ta'dhim are introduced. Then read the translation to grasp the core idea.
Pay attention to the example 'majadtu an-naqah' which illustrates the action-oriented meaning of tamjid.
2
Analyze the derivation
The commentator explains that tamjid is derived from 'majd' which means generosity manifested in actions. This contrasts with ta'dhim, which is linked to essential perfections. Understand the linguistic basis for the distinction.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself why it matters to distinguish between active and essential perfections. This distinction helps in properly affirming Allah's attributes without confusion.
This connects to the broader topic of kalam and how we speak about Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتمجيده بإثبات الكمالات الفعلية) خص التمجيد بإثبات الكمالات الفعلية، لأنه مأخوذ من المجد وهو الكرم المشعر بالآثار والأفعال، ويقال : مجدت الناقة أي علفتها ففيه أيضاً ملاحظة الإعطاء والفعل وخص التعظيم بإثبات الكمالات الوضعية الذاتية بقرينة المقابلة والتقديم، وحملاً على الإفادة ثم إنه فصل فيما يتعلق بالقوة النظرية لاتفاق شرائع المرسلين عليه، وأجمل فيما يتعلق بالقوة العملية أعني الأحكام الفرعية لاختلافهم في تفصيلها.
His saying: 'And glorifying Him by affirming the active perfections.' He specifically assigned tamjid to affirming the active perfections because it is derived from majd, which is generosity that indicates effects and actions. It is said: 'majadtu an-naqah' meaning I fed her, so in it there is also the consideration of giving and action. And he specifically assigned ta'dhim to affirming the essential (or perhaps positional? الوضعية) perfections based on the context of contrast and priority, and as a useful usage. Then he detailed regarding the theoretical power due to the agreement of the laws of the messengers upon it, and he summarized regarding the practical power, i.e., the subsidiary rulings, due to their difference in their details.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage explains the specific use of two terms: ta'dhim (magnification) and tamjid (glorification). The commentator notes that tamjid is specifically applied to affirming active perfections because the root 'majd' suggests generosity that manifests in effects and actions, as illustrated by the Arabic expression 'majadtu an-naqah' meaning 'I fed the she-camel' which involves giving and action. In contrast, ta'dhim is reserved for affirming essential (or 'positional'?) perfections, based on the context of the text. The commentator then observes that the prophetic message is detailed (fassala) regarding matters of theoretical power because all prophets agree on principles, but it is summarized (ajmala) regarding practical power, i.e., subsidiary rulings, because the details vary among different communities.

Key Terms
تمجيد
تعظيم
كمالات فعلية
كمالات ذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tamjid and ta'dhim are synonyms used interchangeably for all perfections.
Correction: Tamjid is specifically for active perfections (derived from 'majd' implying action), while ta'dhim is for essential perfections (inherent attributes).

Checkpoint
Why is tamjid specifically linked to active perfections?
A) Because it is derived from the word for generosity implying action
B) Because tamjid is only used for prophets
C) Because active perfections are more important
D) Because the text says so arbitrarily

Key Takeaway
Tamjid is used for active perfections due to its root meaning of generosity in action, while ta'dhim is used for essential perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you utter 'Subhanallah' or 'Allahu Akbar', do you consciously reflect on whether you are praising Allah for His active perfections or His essential perfections? How might this awareness change the quality of your dhikr starting today?
?
Reflect on a time you praised Allah for something He gave you. Was your focus on the giving (tamjid) or on the Giver's greatness (ta'dhim)? Are you missing one aspect? Commit to balancing both in your next prayer.
?
If someone asked you why we say 'Al-Majid' (The Glorious) and not 'Al-'Adhim' (The Magnificent) in certain contexts, could you explain the difference based on this lesson? What does that say about your own understanding of Allah's names?

Prophetic Teachings: Detailed Theory, Summarized Practice
Prophetic teachings detailed in theoretical power, summarized in practical power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the prophetic message provides detailed guidance on theoretical matters of faith but gives summarized rulings on practical matters.

Core Idea
Prophets detail theory; they summarize practice.

The commentator observes a pattern in the prophetic message: matters related to the theoretical power (intellect and belief) are explained in detail because all prophets agree on them, while matters of practical power (subsidiary rulings) are summarized because the details differ among communities. This insight helps us appreciate the universality of core beliefs and the flexibility of Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the contrast between 'fassala' (detailed) and 'ajmala' (summarized).
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage mentions 'theoretical power' (al-quwwa an-nazariyya) and 'practical power' (al-quwwa al-amaliyya). Understand what each refers to: theoretical power deals with beliefs and principles, practical power deals with actions and rulings.
3
Understand the reason
The reason for detailing theory is the agreement of all prophetic laws. The reason for summarizing practice is the difference in details among laws. Reflect on how this shows the wisdom of Islamic law being both universal and adaptable.
This is a key insight in comparative jurisprudence.

The Meaning of 'Milla'
Explains the linguistic origin of 'milla' from stitching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of 'milla' and its connection to the concept of gathering or stitching.

Core Idea
'Milla' comes from stitching, meaning it gathers people together under one religion.

The commentary opens by examining the word 'milla'. Understanding its root helps us appreciate why it is used for the prophetic community. The author traces it back to stitching a garment, highlighting the sense of collection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on 'milla'
Read the first part of the source_arabic that discusses 'milla'. Note the words 'ملة' and 'مللت الثوب'.
The phrase 'قوله : (تسمى ملة' indicates a gloss on the term.
2
Understand the derivation
The passage says 'milla' is from 'mallaltu al-thawb', meaning to stitch a garment. This implies the idea of bringing together or collecting.
Contrast this with 'kitabah' (writing) which also has a sense of collection but is from 'imlal' (dictation).
3
Reflect on the meaning
Consider why 'stitching' is a fitting metaphor for a religious community: it gathers diverse individuals into one fabric.
This is a lesson from classical Arabic lexicography.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (تسمى ملة الظاهر أنه من مللت الثوب بمعنى خطته وفيه معنى الجمع وأما الكتابة التي فيها معنى الجمع أيضاً فالمشهور أنها الإملال كذا يفهم من الصحاح. قوله : (وأسدهم أصوبهم (قبلةً الوجه في أنه عليه السلام أصوب قبلة بالنسبة إلى إبراهيم :
His saying: 'It is called milla. Apparently it is from 'mallaltu al-thawb' meaning I stitched it, and in it is the meaning of collection. As for 'kitabah' which also has the meaning of collection, the well-known opinion is that it is from 'imlal'. This is understood from al-Sihah. His saying: 'And the most correct of them in direction (qibla)' the aspect is that he (peace be upon him) is more correct in direction (qibla) relative to Ibrahim:
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage comprises two separate glosses. The first explains the term 'milla' (community/religion). It says the apparent derivation is from 'mallaltu al-thawb' (I stitched a garment), implying the meaning of bringing together or collecting. It notes that the verb 'kitabah' (writing) also carries the sense of collection, and the famous opinion is that it is from 'imlal' (dictation), as understood from the lexicon al-Sihah. The second gloss begins to explain the phrase 'and the most correct of them in direction (qibla)' by stating that the Prophet's qibla is more correct than even Prophet Ibrahim's. However, the text cuts off before completing the explanation. The implication is that the Ka'bah, which is the qibla of Muhammad, was built by Ibrahim, but the direction itself is more correctly oriented in the context of the Prophet's community due to the restoration of the original tawhidic orientation.

Correction: The passage clarifies that 'milla' is from 'mallaltu al-thawb' meaning to stitch, emphasizing the concept of gathering.

Clearing a Misunderstanding: Al-Ash'ari on Eternal Meaning
Commentator corrects a common mistake about al-Ash'ari's view on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ambiguity of the term 'maʿnā' (meaning) led to a mistaken attribution to al-Ashʿarī regarding the temporality of Quranic expressions.

Core Idea
A single ambiguous word can cause a centuries-old theological misunderstanding.

You may have heard that al-Ashʿarī considered the recited Quran to be created. The commentator shows that this claim rests on a simple linguistic confusion. By examining this passage, we see how the word 'meaning' (maʿnā) has two senses, and how a transmitter's misinterpretation led to a false attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text aloud, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: 'قديم', 'معنى', and 'عبارات حادثة'.
The passage begins by reporting the famous view and then immediately corrects it.
2
Identify the two senses of 'maʿnā'
The commentator explains that 'maʿnā' can mean either 'that which is opposed to expression' (i.e., internal speech) or 'a quality subsisting in something else'. Understand both meanings.
This ambiguity is the core of the confusion.
3
Understand the transmitter's error
The transmitter took 'maʿnā' in the first sense (internal speech) and thus assumed that al-Ashʿarī considered the expressions (the recited words) to be temporal. The commentator insists this was a mistake.
The Shaykh did not explicitly state the temporality of expressions.
4
Reflect on the commentator's correction
The passage promises further clarity later. For now, note that the correction rests on distinguishing the two senses of 'maʿnā' and realizing that al-Ashʿarī's statement was about the eternal meaning subsisting in God, not about the expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما اشتهر عن الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري من أن القديم معنى قائم بذاته تعالى قد عبر عنه بهذه العبارات الحادثة، فقد قيل : إنه غلط من الناقل منشؤه اشتراك لفظ المعنى بين ما يقابل وبين ما يقوم بغيره. وسيزداد ذلك وضوحاً فيما بعد إن شاء الله تعالى. قوله : (منشؤه اشتراك لفظ المعنى إلخ يريد أن الشيخ قال : إِن القديم هو معنى قائم بذاته تعالى ففهم الناقل من لفظ المعنى ما يقابل اللفظ أعني الكلام النفسي، وتوهم لذلك أن العبارات حادثة عند الشيخ فنقل كما فهم لا أن الشيخ صرح بحدوث العبارات.
And as for what is famous about Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, that the eternal is a meaning subsisting in His Essence, the Exalted, which was expressed by these temporal expressions, it has been said: it is a mistake of the transmitter, its source being the ambiguity of the word 'meaning' (maʿnā) between that which is opposed to 'expression' and that which subsists in something else. This will become clearer later, God willing. His statement: 'Its source is the ambiguity of the word meaning etc.' means that the Shaykh said: the eternal is a meaning subsisting in His Essence, so the transmitter understood from the word 'meaning' that which is opposed to 'expression', i.e., internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī), and therefore imagined that expressions are temporal according to the Shaykh, so he transmitted as he understood, not that the Shaykh explicitly stated the temporality of expressions.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and correction of the famous view attributed to Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī. The commentator reports that it is widely transmitted that al-Ashʿarī said the eternal (qadīm) is a meaning (maʿnā) subsisting in God's Essence, expressed through temporal expressions. However, this transmission is a mistake by the transmitter, caused by the ambiguity of the term 'meaning' (maʿnā). In Arabic, 'meaning' can refer either to what is opposite to 'expression' (i.e., internal speech, kalām nafsī) or to a quality that subsists in something else. The transmitter took the word in the first sense and thus concluded that al-Ashʿarī considered the expressions (ʿibārāt) to be temporal. In reality, al-Ashʿarī did not explicitly state that expressions are temporal; the transmitter merely inferred it. The commentator promises further clarification later. The second paragraph (a marginal note) reiterates that the transmitter misunderstood the term 'meaning' and assumed al-Ashʿarī held expressions to be temporal, when in fact al-Ashʿarī's statement was about the eternal meaning subsisting in God, not about the expressions being temporal.

Key Terms
أبو الحسن الأشعري
قديم
معنى
كلام نفسي
عبارات حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ashʿarī held that the recited Quran (the expressions) are temporal (created).
Correction: This is a mistake by a transmitter who misunderstood the term 'maʿnā'. Al-Ashʿarī spoke of an eternal meaning subsisting in God; he did not state that the expressions are temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why did the transmitter mistakenly attribute to al-Ashʿarī the view that the expressions of the Quran are temporal?
A) The transmitter misunderstood al-Ashʿarī's argument.
B) The term 'meaning' (maʿnā) is ambiguous between internal speech and a subsisting attribute.
C) Al-Ashʿarī later changed his position.
D) The transmitter was a heretic.

Key Takeaway
The widespread attribution to al-Ashʿarī that Quranic expressions are temporal is a misinterpretation arising from the ambiguous term 'maʿnā'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever confidently repeated a claim about a scholar or a belief without verifying its source? How might that have misled others? What will you do the next time you hear a controversial claim?
?
This passage shows that a single ambiguous word led to a false attribution that lasted for centuries. Are there any beliefs you hold that might be based on a misunderstanding of a term or a misquotation? How will you seek clarity?
?
The commentator corrects this misunderstanding with care and evidence. Are you as careful when you speak about God and His attributes? Will you commit to studying the original sources before repeating theological statements?

Definition and Origin of Khurafa
Defines 'khurafa' and traces its etymological origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical meaning of the term 'khurafa' in Islamic theology and its linguistic origin.

Core Idea
'Khurafa' is any report with no basis; its name comes from a man kidnapped by jinn.

The commentator defines the term 'khurafa' as used in the phrase 'with the fabricated tales of the people of innovation.' This passage clarifies that the word originally referred to the incredible stories of a specific person which were dismissed as false, and later became a general term for any baseless report. Understanding this term helps us evaluate claims attributed to innovators.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the phrase 'بخرافات أهل البدع'. The commentator is glossing this term.
The Arabic uses the plural 'خرافات' from the root 'خ ر ف'.
2
Understand the definition
The passage says: 'الخرافة كل حديث لا أصل له' – khurāfa is every report that has no basis. 'أصل' here means a chain of transmission or a sound foundation.
3
Learn the origin story
It traces the word to a man named Khurāfa who was kidnapped by jinn. He would narrate what he saw, and people accused him of lying, calling his tales 'حديث خرافة'. Later it became a general term.
This is a well-known etymological story in Arabic.
4
See the shift in usage
Originally a specific phrase, it became a general term for any baseless report. This shows how language evolves.
5
Reflect on theological use
In the context of the text, 'خرافات أهل البدع' disparages the teachings of innovators as baseless fabrications.
This term is a polemical tool in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( بخرافات أهل البدع الخرافة كل حديث لا أصل له وأصله أن رجلاً اسمه خرافة استهوته الجن فكان يحدث بما رأي فكذبوه وقالوا : حديث خرافة ثم أطلقوه على كل حديث لا أصل له .
His saying: 'With the خرافات (fabricated tales) of the people of innovation. Al-khurāfa is every report that has no basis. Its origin is that a man named Khurāfa was kidnapped by the jinn and he used to relate what he saw, so they accused him of lying and said: 'The tale of Khurāfa.' Then they applied it to every report that has no basis.'
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a gloss that defines the term 'خرافة' (khurāfa) as used in the phrase 'بخرافات أهل البدع' (with the fabricated tales of the people of innovation). It explains that the word originally referred to stories of a specific person named Khurāfa who was taken by the jinn and later recounted improbable events, which people dismissed as false. Over time, the term came to be applied to any baseless report. Thus, the passage clarifies a key term used in theological polemic against innovators.

Key Terms
خرافة
أهل البدع
حديث لا أصل له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Khurāfa means any kind of myth or fable.
Correction: In Islamic terminology, khurāfa specifically refers to a report that has no chain of transmission or basis (lā aṣla lah). It is not a general synonym for 'myth' but a technical term in hadith criticism.

Checkpoint
What is the origin of the term 'خرافة'?
A) It is a derivative of 'خرف' meaning senility.
B) It comes from a man named Khurafa who was taken by jinn.
C) It refers to tall tales told by travelers.
D) It means innovation.

Key Takeaway
The term 'khurafa' in Islamic theology refers to any report lacking a valid chain of transmission, originating from the tale of a man named Khurafa.

First and Second Perfection Defined
Definition of first and second perfection for any species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between first perfection (the actualization of a species) and second perfection (the acquisition of attributes and effects).

Core Idea
A species' perfection has two levels: first the essence, then its attributes and effects.

This lesson introduces a foundational concept in kalam and philosophy: the two levels of perfection. The author, al-Sanusi, explains that every species has a first perfection, which is its actualization through its specific difference, and a second perfection, which is the realization of its particular attributes and intended effects. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing the nature of beings, especially in discussions about Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the two perfections
Notice how the author defines first perfection as the actualization of the species, and second perfection as the acquisition of attributes and effects.
3
Connect to an example
Think of an example, like a human: the rational soul is the first perfection, while knowledge and virtue are the second perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كمال كل نوع يعني أن كماله بعد تحصله وتكمله نوعاً بمنوعه المسمى كمالاً أول على الإطلاق إنما هو بحصول صفاته الخاصة به، وصدور آثاره المقصودة منه ويسمى هذا الكمال كمالاً ثانياً
For the perfection of every species means that its perfection after its actualization and completion as a species by its specific difference, called first perfection in an absolute sense, is only through the acquisition of its particular attributes and the emanation of its intended effects from it, and this perfection is called second perfection.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines two levels of perfection (كمال) for any species. The first perfection (الكمال الأول) is the actualization of the species itself through its specific difference (المنوع / الفصل). The second perfection (الكمال الثاني) is the attainment of the particular attributes that belong to that species and the production of the effects intended from it. For example, for a human, the first perfection is the rational soul (النفس الناطقة), and the second perfection is the acquisition of knowledge and virtue. This distinction is foundational in kalam and philosophy for understanding the fulfillment of a thing's nature.

آثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: First perfection is the same as second perfection.
Correction: They are distinct: first is the essential actualization, second is the actualization of attributes and effects.

Checkpoint
What is the first perfection of a species according to this definition?
A) Its particular attributes
B) Its intended effects
C) Its actualization through its specific difference
D) Its existence in the mind

Key Takeaway
The perfection of any species consists of a first perfection (its essential actualization) and a second perfection (its attributes and effects).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
As a human being, your first perfection is your rational soul. But are you actively cultivating your second perfection—the attributes and effects that should flow from it? What specific attribute or effect from this lesson are you neglecting right now, and what will you do about it starting today?
?
If you were to die today, would you consider yourself truly perfected according to the definition in this passage? Your soul exists, but what about the intended effects? Name one effect you want to leave on this world, and commit to one step toward it this week.
?
The passage says second perfection is 'only through' attributes and effects. Does your daily routine reflect that belief, or do you act as if simply being alive as a Muslim is enough? What is one attribute you know you lack, and how will you acquire it?

Critique of the Definition of Perfection
Critique of perfection definition with justification of terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of classifying perfection into first and second, the justification for calling essential properties 'attributes', and the specific problem with the human rational soul as a specifying form.

Core Idea
Perfection includes both first and second; calling essential properties 'attributes' is a convenience that fails for external parts.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif delivers a sharp critique of the earlier definition of perfection. The commentator objects to restricting 'perfection' to second perfection and traces the confusion back to al-Abhari. He then explains the theological reasoning behind calling essential properties 'attributes', but warns that this reasoning only applies to logical parts, not external ones. Finally, he raises a profound difficulty: the rational soul, being immaterial, cannot serve as a specifying form for the human species.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to follow the critic's argument from beginning to end. Notice the shift from critique to justification to a new problem.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the critic's objection
The critic argues that limiting perfection to second perfection is unnecessary. Find the evidence he gives (the example of human rational faculty) and his accusation of misunderstanding terminology.
He says the author neglected that essential properties are called psychic attributes.
3
Understand al-Abhari's role
The critic traces the origin to al-Abhari, who distinguished first perfections (differentiating species) from second perfections (emanation of effects). This is key to the dispute.
4
Grasp the justification and its limitation
The passage explains why essential properties are called attributes: difficulty of accessing real parts of quiddities, so they used near effects. But note that this only applies to predicable parts, not external parts.
The commentator then adds the problem with the human rational soul as a specifying form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويسمى كمالاً ثانياً) قيل : حمل الكمال المذكور على الكمال الثاني، ليحتاج إلى تقييد النوع المذكور بقوله : بعد تحصيله وتكمله إلخ لا يحتاج إليه، بل لا طائل تحته لأن تمثيل الكمال المقصود بالنسبة إلى الإنسان بالقوة النطقية وما يتبعها من العقل مناد بأعلى صوته، على أن المراد بالكمال مطلق الأمور المختصة سواء كان أولاً أو ثانياً، وكأنه فهم ذلك من لفظ الصفات فإنها تكون خارجةً، وقد غفل عن اصطلاحهم أن الذاتيات تسمى الصفات النفسية، وأقول : أصل هذا مأخوذ من كلام الأبهري حيث حمل قوله بحصول صفاتها على الكمالات الأولى المنوعة وقوله بصدور آثارها على الكمالات الثانية، ووجه إطلاق الصفة على الذاتيات بأن أجزاء الماهيات الحقيقية يتعذر الاطلاع عليها، أو يتعسر فأخذوا الأثر القريب الذي يستتبع سائر الآثار المختصة بالنوع، وسموه فصلاً والأثر العام القريب الذي يستتبع سائر الآثار العامة له، جنساً تسهيلاً للطالب، وصح تسمية الذاتي بهذا الاعتبار صفة له. قال : وهذا هو السبب في إطلاق المتكلمين الصفة على الذاتيات وأنت خبير بأن ما ذكره إنما يستقيم في الأجزاء المحمولة المسماة بالذاتيات وأما الأجزاء الخارجية فلا يطلق عليها الصفات النفسية، ولا يتعسر الاطلاع عليها، نعم قد يحمل الصورة المنوعة في بعض الأنواع كالإنسان على ما عرف، حتى قيل : إن النفس الناطقة ليست صورة منوعة له لأنها مجردةٌ فكيف تكون صورة منوعة
His saying: 'and this perfection is called second perfection.' It is said: Applying the aforementioned perfection to the second perfection requires restricting the mentioned species by his saying 'after its actualization and completion' etc., but it is not needed. Rather, there is no benefit in it because the example of the intended perfection with respect to man – the rational faculty and what follows it from intellect – calls out with a loud voice. What is meant by perfection is absolute particular matters, whether first or second. And it seems he understood that from the word 'attributes' because they are external. And he neglected their terminology that essential properties are called 'psychic attributes' (al-sifat al-nafsiyya). And I say: The origin of this is taken from the speech of al-Abhari where he applied his saying 'by the acquisition of its attributes' to the first perfections that differentiate species, and his saying 'by the emanation of its effects' to the second perfections. And the justification for applying 'attribute' to essential properties is that the parts of real quiddities are either impossible to access or difficult, so they took the near effect that entails the rest of the effects specific to the species and called it a differentia (fasl), and the near general effect that entails the rest of its general effects they called a genus (jins), as a facilitation for the student. So it is correct to call the essential property an 'attribute' in this consideration. He said: This is the reason for the theologians' application of 'attribute' to essential properties. And you are well aware that what he mentioned is only applicable to the predicable parts called essential properties, but as for the external parts, they are not called psychic attributes, nor is access to them difficult. Yes, the specifying form (al-sura al-munawwi'a) may be attributed in some species like man as is known, until it is said that the rational soul is not a specifying form for him because it is immaterial; so how can it be a specifying form?
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a critical gloss (قوله) on the preceding definition of perfection. The critic argues that applying the term 'perfection' only to the second perfection is unnecessary and problematic. He claims that the example of man's first perfection (rational faculty) clearly shows that what is meant by perfection is absolute particular matters, whether first or second. The critic accuses the author of misunderstanding that essential properties (الذاتيات) are called 'psychic attributes' (الصفات النفسية) in their terminology. He traces the origin of this error to al-Abhari, who supposedly applied the phrase 'acquisition of its attributes' to first perfections (differentiating species) and 'emanation of its effects' to second perfections. The commentator then explains the justification for calling essential properties 'attributes': since the parts of real quiddities are difficult to access, scholars used the near effect that implies the rest of the specific effects as a differentia (فصل), and the near general effect as a genus (جنس), making it permissible to call the essential property 'attribute' in that sense. However, the commentator points out that this only applies to predicable parts (essential properties in the logical sense), not to external parts. He raises a further difficulty: for species like man, the specifying form (الصورة المنوعة) – the rational soul – is immaterial, so it cannot be a specifying form (since specifying forms are usually material), challenging the standard definition of the human species.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'perfection' in kalam refers only to second perfection (acquired attributes), and that essential properties cannot be called attributes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that perfection includes both first and second perfections, and that essential properties are technically called 'psychic attributes' due to a conventional justification based on near effects.

Checkpoint
According to the critic in this passage, why did the theologians call essential properties 'attributes'?
A) Because they are external to the essence.
B) Because access to the real parts of quiddities is difficult, so they used near effects as differentiae and genera.
C) Because they are attributes of action.
D) Because the rational soul is an attribute.

Key Takeaway
The definition of perfection is contested: it includes both first and second, and the technical use of 'attribute' for essential properties is a pragmatic convention with limits.

Are you using it to know Him, or is it occupied with trivialities? Today, examine one thought you entertained: was it a true effect of your rational soul or a mere distraction?
?
The passage reveals that even scholars used approximations (near effects) to define essences. You often judge yourself and others by external actions (second perfections), but have you neglected the inner essence (first perfection)? What would it mean for you to seek the 'near effect' of your faith—not just rituals, but the very faculty of belief and reason that makes you human? The commentator challenges the notion that the rational soul can be a specifying form because it is immaterial. Your soul is not bound by matter; it is a direct sign of Allah's creative power. Critique of al-Abhari's Perfections
Criticizes al-Abhari's definition and application of the two perfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections to al-Abhari's classification of perfections and why the first perfection cannot be used as a basis for attributing qualities.

Core Idea
Al-Abhari's definition of perfections is critiqued for essential inconsistencies.

In this passage, the author systematically dismantles al-Abhari’s formulation of the two perfections. You will see how logical gaps and misapplications force a rethinking of how perfections relate to attributes. This critique is a classic example of rigorous argumentation in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the author's critique unfold naturally.
2
Identify the first criticism
Notice that al-Abhari's formulation fails to address the first division of the second perfection. The author suggests generalizing attributes as a better approach.
This is the initial logical gap.
3
Understand why first perfection cannot be used
The first perfection is the specific differentia, so applying attributes to it via the mentioned justification is improper. Grasp why this is a key flaw.
4
Examine the variation argument
Al-Abhari's phrase 'by increase and decrease' indicates he is not referring to first perfections because they do not vary. Forcing the reference is a distortion.
This highlights a misuse of terminology.
5
Recognize the final clue
Al-Abhari's own statement about 'perfection in comprehending intelligibles' shows he initially meant the second perfection, not the first.
This ties the critique together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن فيما ذكره الأبهري لزوم عدم التعرض للقسم الأول من الكمال الثاني، وأقرب منه تعميم الصفات إياها. وأيضاً الكمال الأول المنوع في نفس الأمر فلا يحسن إطلاق الصفات عليه بالتوجيه المذكور. ثم إن قوله: وبحسب زيادة ذلك ونقصانه يأبى عن حمل الصفات على الكمالات الأولى، إذ الكمال الأول لا يتفاوت في أشخاص النوع، وجعل الإشارة إلى ما سواه تعسف ظاهر. وأيضاً قوله: فأذن كماله بتعقل المعقولات يدل على أن المراد بالكمال المذكور أولاً هو الكمال الثاني كما لا يخفى.
Moreover, in what al-Abhari mentioned there is a necessary lack of addressing the first division of the second perfection, and closer than it is the generalization of the attributes to them. Also the first perfection is the specific differentia in reality, so it is not proper to apply attributes to it by the mentioned justification. Then his saying: 'And by the increase and decrease of that' prevents attributing the attributes to the first perfections, since the first perfection does not vary among the individuals of the species, and making the reference to other than it is manifest distortion. And also his saying: 'Then his perfection is in comprehending intelligibles' indicates that what is meant by the perfection mentioned first is the second perfection, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage contains a critique of al-Abhari's treatment of perfections. The author points out several problems. First, al-Abhari's formulation necessarily fails to address the first division of the second perfection. A better approach would be to generalize the attributes to cover both divisions. Second, the first perfection is itself the specific differentia in each species, so applying attributes to it via the mentioned justification is improper. Third, al-Abhari's use of the phrase 'by the increase and decrease of that' shows that he is not referring to the first perfections, because the first perfection does not vary among individuals of a species. Forcing the reference to something else is a clear distortion. Finally, al-Abhari's own statement that 'his perfection is in comprehending intelligibles' proves that the perfection referred to initially is the second perfection, not the first.

Key Terms
الأبهري
الكمال الأول
الكمال الثاني
التعسف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first perfection can vary and be the basis for attributing qualities like praise.
Correction: The first perfection does not vary among individuals of a species, so it cannot serve as a basis for varying attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject applying attributes to the first perfection?
A) Because the first perfection does not vary among individuals.
B) Because the first perfection is not real.
C) Because al-Abhari said so.
D) Because attributes only apply to the second perfection.

Key Takeaway
Al-Abhari's treatment of perfections suffers from multiple logical flaws, particularly in confusing the first and second perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate your own worth, do you tend to confuse your fixed nature (first perfection) with your achievements (second perfection)? How does this confusion affect your humility and reliance on Allah? Commit to distinguishing the two in your self-reflection from today.

Al-Abhari's error was treating the first perfection as variable. Is there an area of your life where you treat something unchangeable about yourself as if it could be improved through effort, or vice versa? How does this distort your relationship with Allah? What step will you take this week to align your perspective with the truth?
?
The critique in this lesson shows the danger of carelessly applying terms. In your own speech about yourself or others, are there terms you use loosely that lead to self-deception or pride? Ask Allah for precision in your understanding and for a heart that acknowledges its true fixed nature before Him.
