Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the unknown face is completely unknown, making seeking impossible.
Correction: The unknown face is not completely unknown; it is known through its union with the known face, thus seeking is possible without a third entity.

Checkpoint
According to the earlier scholars, why is the unknown face not absolutely unknown?
A) Because it is known in itself.
B) Because it is known through its union with the known face.
C) Because it is the same as the known face.
D) Because it is known by a third thing.

Key Takeaway
The earlier scholars solve the shubha by showing that the unknown face is known through its union with the known face, so no third entity is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to know Allah, do you rely on the 'known faces' He has revealed (like His names and attributes) or do you demand a direct vision of His essence? How does this lesson challenge you to trust the known faces He has given?
?
Are there aspects of your faith that you consider 'unknown' and thus avoid pursuing? How can you use the known faces (the Qur'an and Sunnah) to access that knowledge, and what step will you take this week to do so?
?
The earlier scholars refused to invent a 'third thing' to solve the shubha. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes create intermediaries or innovations because you feel the known path is insufficient? Where might you be adding unnecessary 'third things' instead of relying on what Allah has already made known?

The Later Scholars' Answer and the Instrument of Observation
Answer to the shubha from the later scholars' perspective with the instrument analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) answer the shubha by distinguishing between seeking the two faces and seeking the thing itself, using the concept of an instrument (āla) of observation.

Core Idea
The thing itself can be sought via an instrument, even if the faces cannot be sought directly.

Unlike the earlier scholars, the later scholars accept that the two faces cannot be sought individually, but they argue that the impossibility of seeking the faces does not prevent seeking the thing that possesses those faces. They introduce a powerful analogy: the known face functions as an instrument that makes the thing observable. In this lesson, we explore how this instrument-based approach resolves the shubha without needing to deny that the unknown face is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Read the later scholars' answer
Read the Arabic passage starting with 'وإن أجيب على رأي المتأخرين' and its translation. Notice the key claim: impossibility of seeking the two faces does not entail impossibility of seeking the third thing.
The 'third thing' here is the thing itself (the possessor of the faces).
2
Understand the instrument analogy
The passage explains that the known face becomes an instrument (āla) and a mirror for the thing. Similarly, the thing can be sought by making another accident an instrument. This allows the thing to be known from one aspect and unknown from another without contradiction.
The instrument is not sought itself; it is used to seek the thing.
3
Reflect on the unity of known and unknown
The known and unknown are not contradictory because they relate to different aspects: the thing is known by one face and unknown by another. This is a key insight of the later school.
This resolves the shubha without requiring a major overhaual of the manṭiq framework.

If it is answered according to the view of the earlier scholars, then the answer is what the author (al-Misri?) mentioned, namely that we do not concede that the unknown face is absolutely unknown, because if the known face is known in terms of its union with that thing, and the unknown is unknown from that aspect, then the unknown face is known in terms of the union of the known face with it, and there is no meaning then for the answer of Naqd al-Muhassal, since the sought-object is not a third thing according to them. ?

In your life, have you ever avoided seeking truth about yourself because you were afraid of what you might find? Refuting the Objection on the Imam's Statement
Refutation of a commentator's objection against the Imam's statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's objection against the Imam's statement about the union of the two faces and how the author refutes it by clarifying that the union does not imply essential distinction.

Core Idea
The Imam's 'union in one thing' refers to the whole, not an essential distinction.

After presenting the two answers, the author addresses a commentator (likely al-Sharif) who raised an objection against the Imam. The commentator claimed that the Imam's acknowledgment of a distinction between the two faces and their possessor undermines the later scholars' solution. In this lesson, we examine the author's careful refutation: the Imam's phrase 'when they are united in one thing' only indicates a distinction between container and contained, not an essential distinction. Without essential distinction, the objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's objection and the author's response
Read the Arabic passage starting with 'وأما ما ذكره الشارح' and its translation. Identify the commentator's claim: the Imam's statement implies a distinction that would allow for seeking a third thing.
The commentator argues that the Imam's phrase 'when they are united in one thing' shows recognition of distinction.
2
Analyze the author's counterargument
The author argues that the phrase only indicates distinction between container and contained (e.g., the thing and its faces), not essential distinction. He suggests interpreting 'one thing' as the whole, not a separate entity.
The key is that the union is of container and contained, not of two separate essences.
3
Understand the consequence
If the distinction is not essential, then the objection fails. The two faces are not separate entities, so there is no third thing to seek. The later scholars' solution stands.
This protects the integrity of the later scholars' answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أجيب علي رأي المتقدمين فالجواب ما ذكره المصنف، وهو أنا لا نسلم أن الوجه المجهول مجهول مطلقاً، لأنه إذا كان الوجه المعلوم معلوماً من حيث الاتحاد بذلك الشيء، والمجهول مجهولاً من تلك الحيثية كان الوجه المجهول معلوماً من حيث اتحاد الوجه المعلوم به ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب نقد المحصل إذ المطلوب ليس أمرا ثالثا عندهم، وإن أجيب على رأي المتأخرين فالجواب ما ذكره في النقد وهو أنه لا يلزم من امتناع طلب الوجهين امتناع طلب الأمر الثالث الذي هو ذو الوجهين، فكما أن الوجه المعلوم صار آلة الملاحظة الشيء، ومرآة لانكشافه كذلك، يطلب ذلك الشيء بأن يصير أمر آخر آلة لملاحظته، ومرآة له وتفصيله أن عارض الشيء قد يلاحظ في نفسه، فيكون العارض معلوماً، والشيء مغفولاً عنه بالكلية، وقد يجعل آلة لملاحظته، وحينئذ يكون معلوماً باعتبار ذلك العارض مجهولاً باعتبار آخر، فيتحد المعلوم والمجهول لكنه معلوم من حيثية ومجهول من حيثية أخرى، ولا استمالة فيه ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب المصنف، إذ ليس المطلوب عندهم الوجه حتى يجاب بأن الوجه المجهول ليس مجهولاً مطلقاً، فتدبر والله الموفق وأما ما ذكره الشارح من أنه الزام للإمام حيث اعترف بمغايرة الوجهين لذي الوجهين ففيه إن العبارة المشعرة بالتغاير ليس إلا قوله : لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد، إذ لا بد من التغاير بين الظرف والمظروف، وهو لا يقتضي التغاير بالذات لجواز أن يكون مراده لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد هو الكل من حيث هو كل بل نقول : لا بد من حمل كلامه على ذلك، إذ لو حمل على التغاير بالذات لم يتم التقريب إذ لا يلزم من انتفاء الإجمال في الوجهين انتفاء الإجمال في الشيء ذي الوجهين وحينئذ لا يتم الإلزام إذ ليس في الحقيقة إلا الوجهان، ولا يمكن طلب شيء منهما .
If it is answered according to the view of the earlier scholars, then the answer is what the author (al-Misri?) mentioned, namely that we do not concede that the unknown face is absolutely unknown, because if the known face is known in terms of its union with that thing, and the unknown is unknown from that aspect, then the unknown face is known in terms of the union of the known face with it, and there is no meaning then for the answer of Naqd al-Muhassal, since the sought-object is not a third thing according to them. And if it is answered according to the view of the later scholars, then the answer is what is mentioned in al-Naqd, namely that the impossibility of seeking the two faces does not entail the impossibility of seeking the third thing which is the possessor of the two faces. For just as the known face became an instrument for observing the thing and a mirror for its disclosure, likewise that thing is sought by becoming another thing an instrument for its observation and a mirror for it. And its detail is that an accident of a thing may be observed in itself, so the accident becomes known and the thing is completely disregarded; and it may be made an instrument for observing it, and at that time it becomes known in respect of that accident and unknown in another respect, so the known and unknown unite, but it is known from one aspect and unknown from another aspect; and there is no improbability in it, and there is no meaning then for the answer of the author, because the sought-object according to them is not the face such that it is answered that the unknown face is not absolutely unknown. So reflect, and God is the grantor of success. And as for what the commentator (al-Sharif?) mentioned, that it is an objection to the Imam where he acknowledged the distinction of the two faces from the possessor of the two faces, there is in it that the expression indicating distinction is only his saying: 'but when they are united in one thing', for there must be distinction between the container and the contained, and this does not entail distinction in essence (bi al-dhat), because it is possible that his intention by 'when they are united in one thing' is the whole in terms of being a whole; rather we say: it is necessary to interpret his words in that way, because if it were interpreted as distinction in essence, the approximation would not be complete, since the negation of ambiguity in the two faces does not necessitate the negation of ambiguity in the thing possessing the two faces, and then the objection is not complete, for in reality there are only the two faces, and it is not possible to seek anything of them.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct answers to the shubha (sophistical argument) about seeking something known by one face and unknown by another, depending on which school's view of 'ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh and 'ilm wajh al-shay is adopted. First, according to the earlier scholars (mutaqaddimun), the answer is that the unknown face is not absolutely unknown because it is known through its union with the known face, so there is no need for a third thing. This refutes the objection based on Naqd al-Muhassal. Second, according to the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun), the answer is that the impossibility of seeking the two faces does not entail the impossibility of seeking the third thing (the thing itself). The known face serves as an instrument; similarly, the thing can be sought by making another accident an instrument. The author then discusses a point by the commentator (al-Sharif?) that this is an objection to the Imam, but he argues that the Imam's statement about the union of the two faces in one thing does not imply essential distinction; it is merely distinction between container and contained, or referring to the whole as a whole. Thus, the objection fails, and only the two faces exist.

Key Terms
الشارح
الإمام
الوجهان
ذو الوجهين
التغاير
الظرف والمظروف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The commentator thinks that the Imam's mention of 'union in one thing' proves that the faces are essentially distinct from the thing, allowing a third object of seeking.
Correction: The author shows that the union is merely between container and contained, not essential distinction, so no third thing exists.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the Imam's phrase 'when they are united in one thing' properly refer to?
A) Two separate essences united accidentally.
B) The whole as a whole.
C) The two faces as separate substances.
D) The container and the contained as essentially distinct.

Key Takeaway
The commentator's objection fails because the Imam's statement about union indicates a container-contained relationship, not an essential distinction, so no third entity is introduced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a statement from a scholar that seems to imply a position you disagree with, do you first consider the possibility of an alternative interpretation that preserves consistency with their known principles? What prevents you from doing so?
?
In your own learning, are you quick to object before fully grasping the intended meaning? How does this passage challenge that habit?
?
The Imam's statement is defended by showing it can be interpreted in a way that avoids the objection. Is there a time in your life where you assumed the worst interpretation of someone's words and only later realized a more charitable reading? What did you learn from that?

Refutation of the Need for a Third Thing
Refutes the need for a third entity beyond two faces in definition seeking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why no third entity is needed beyond the unknown essence and its known considerations when defining a concept.

Core Idea
Only the unknown essence and its known aspects are needed to seek a concept.

This passage addresses a subtle point in definition logic: when we seek to define an unknown concept, we rely on known aspects of it, but no third entity intervenes. The author refutes the idea that a third thing is needed, using an objection-answer format to clarify the relationship between the unknown essence and its known considerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Note the flow from denial to explanation to objection and answer.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the argument up to that point.
2
Identify the author's main claim
The author asserts that no third thing is needed beyond the unknown essence (the sought concept) and its known considerations (aspects that direct the search). Restate this claim in your own words.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The objection suggests three things arise when seeking 'man' by a face. Find the three things proposed and see how the author reduces them to two, showing the concept itself is the unknown face.
The key move: the concept 'man' is identical to the unknown face, so only two entities remain.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: it prevents unnecessary ontological commitments in definition theory. The note on Naqd al-Muhassal hints at a related debate about summary vs. detailed knowledge.
The argument supports the view that definition involves only the object and its attributes, no intermediary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا حاجة ) في دفع هذه الشبهة إِليه أي إلى إثبات الأمر الثالث لأنها قد اندفعت بما حققناه مع أن إثباته مخالف للواقع، وذلك لأنا إذا أردنا تعريف مفهوم لنتصوره فلا بد أن تكون ذات ذلك المفهوم أي نفسه وعينه مجهولا، وغير حاصل لنا ليمكن تحصيله، وهذا معنى قولنا : المجهول هو الذات أي ذات المطلوب ،وعينه ولا بد هناك أيضاً من أن يكون أمر ما صدق عليه معلوماً ليصح به توجهنا إليه وطلبنا إياه، فهذا هو المراد بقولنا: المعلوم بعض اعتبارات الذات أي بعض اعتبارات ذات المطلوب الذي هو المجهول ولا خفاء في أنه ليس هناك أمر ثالث يتعلق به غرضنا حتى يتصور أن يكون المطلوب أمراً ثالثاً وراء الوجهين فإن قلت : قد يطلب مفهوم الإنسان من حيث هو هو، وقد يطلب وجه من وجوهه، وقد يطلب مفهوم الإنسان بوجه من وجوهه، فعلى هذا التقدير الأخير يثبت أمور ثلاثة مفهوم الإنسان الذي هو المطلوب ووجهه المجهول الذي باعتباره صار مطلوباً ، ووجهه المعلوم الذي به أمكن طلبه قلت : مفهوم الإنسان ذلك الوجه الذي طلب به مفهوم هو المجهول، وهو ذات المطلوب فليس لنا إلا ذات المطلوب المجهول وبعض اعتباراته المعلوم واعلم أن صاحب نقد المحصل أثبت الأمر الثالث إلزاماً للأمام بما ذكره في مسألة المعلوم على الإجمال، حيث قال : المعلوم على سبيل الجملة معلوم من وجه ومجهول من وجه، والوجهان متغايران، والوجه المعلوم لا إِجمال فيه والوجه المجهول غير معلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد ظن أن العلم الجملي نوع يغاير العلم التفصيلي، فإنه قد
His saying: (And there is no need) in repelling this shubha to it, i.e., to the affirmation of the third thing, because it has already been repelled by what we have established, and its affirmation is contrary to reality. That is because when we want to define a concept to conceive it, it is necessary that the essence of that concept, i.e., its self and identity, be unknown and not obtained by us so that it can be obtained. And this is the meaning of our saying: the unknown is the essence, i.e., the essence of the sought-object, and its identity. And there must also be something that is true of it that is known, so that by it our attention to it and our seeking it is valid. This is what is meant by our saying: the known is some of the considerations of the essence, i.e., some of the considerations of the essence of the sought-object which is the unknown. And there is no concealment that there is no third thing to which our purpose attaches, such that it is imagined that the sought-object is a third thing beyond the two faces. For if you say: sometimes the concept of 'man' is sought in itself, sometimes a face of its faces is sought, and sometimes the concept of 'man' is sought by one of its faces; on this last assumption, three things are affirmed: the concept of man which is the sought-object, its unknown face by virtue of which it became sought, and its known face by which its seeking became possible. I say: the concept of man is that face by which the concept is sought is the unknown, and it is the essence of the sought-object; so we have only the unknown essence of the sought-object and some of its known considerations. And know that the author of Naqd al-Muhassal affirmed the third thing as an objection to the Imam by what he mentioned in the issue of the known in a general way, where he said: the known in a summary manner is known by one face and unknown by another, and the two faces are distinct, and the known face has no ambiguity in it, and the unknown face is not known at all; but when they are united in one thing, he thought that summary knowledge is a type different from detailed knowledge, for he has...
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the need for a third thing beyond the two faces. The author (the commentator) first states that the shubha is already repelled by what has been established, and affirming a third thing is contrary to reality. The reason is that when we seek to conceive a concept, its essence must be unknown and not yet obtained, while something that is true of it (a known aspect) must exist to direct our search. There is no third thing involved. The author then addresses an objection: one might think that in seeking 'man' by a face, three things arise (the concept, the unknown face, the known face). The answer is that the concept itself is the unknown face, and the known face is merely a consideration of the essence, so only the unknown essence and its known considerations exist. Finally, the author notes that the writer of Naqd al-Muhassal posited the third thing as an objection to the Imam, based on the idea that summary knowledge (al-'ilm al-ijmali) differs from detailed knowledge (al-'ilm al-tafsili), but the sentence is cut off.

Misconception: That seeking a concept by a face requires a third entity beyond the unknown essence and known aspects.
Correction: The concept itself is the unknown face; only the unknown essence and its known considerations exist—no third thing.

Why 'Musta'bad Jiddan' Instead of 'Ghayr Sahih'?
Commentator explains the choice of 'musta'bad jiddan' based on three meanings of qiyam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three meanings of 'qiyam' and why the claim that the face is a part subsisting in something is only 'very far-fetched' rather than outright false.

Core Idea
The part's subsistence is impossible in one sense, but only far-fetched in two others.

In the original text, the author uses the phrase 'musta'bad jiddan' (very far-fetched) rather than a stronger negation. The commentator explains why: the term 'qiyam' (subsistence) has multiple meanings, and while one is impossible for a part, the other two are only indirectly inapplicable. This distinction reveals the precision of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the commentator's explanation.
2
Identify the three meanings
List the three meanings of qiyam mentioned: (1) occurrence/inherence, (2) attribution as a descriptor, (3) subordination in location.
3
Understand why two are hiddenly false
Reflect on why the second and third meanings are not impossible but their incorrectness is hidden — because they require the part to exist without the whole, which is impossible.
4
Connect to the expression
Notice that the commentator says 'musta'bad jiddan' due to this hiddenness, not 'ghayr sahih'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (مستبعد جداً إذ القيام بمعنى العروض والحصول به ممتنع في الجزء، وأما بمعنى الاختصاص الناعت، أو التبعية في التحيز، فلأنه لا تتصوّر النعتية والتبعية إلا بعد تعقل وجود كل منهما بدون الآخر، ولا يعقل وجود الكل بدون الجزء، ولأجل الخفاء في عدم صحة المعنيين الآخرين، قال: مستبعد جدا دون غير صحيح. قوله : ( ولا خفاء في أنه ليس هناك أمر ثالث قيل : فيه بحث لأن الوجه المعلوم كالماشي بالنسبة إلى الإنسان كنا نعلمه قبل أن يصير آلة لملاحظة أمر ما هو الإنسان، فإذا تصورنا الإنسان بالماشي ففيه ملحوظ، وآلة ملاحظة حاصلة في هذا الآن، ونطلب شيئاً آخر هو آلة ملاحظة أخرى لملحوظنا، ولا فساد في كون الشيء الواحد ملحوظاً بجهتين، والواقع ليس إلا هذا فليتأمل .
His saying: (Musta'bad jiddan) because 'subsistence' (al-qiyam) in the sense of occurrence and inherence in it is impossible in the part; as for in the sense of attribution (al-ikhtisas) as a descriptor (al-na't) or subordination (al-tabi'iyya) in location, it is because descripthood and subordination are not conceivable except after understanding the existence of each without the other, and it is not intelligible for the whole to exist without the part, and due to the hiddenness in the incorrectness of the other two meanings, he said: musta'bad jiddan (very far-fetched) rather than 'ghayr sahih' (not correct). His saying: (And there is no concealment that there is no third thing) it was said: there is investigation in it, because the known face, like 'the walker' in relation to 'man', we know it before it becomes an instrument for observing something which is man. So when we conceive man by 'the walker', there is in it an observed thing and an instrument of observation present at this moment, and we seek another thing that is another instrument of observation for our observed thing; and there is no corruption in one thing being observed by two aspects, and the reality is nothing other than this, so let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage contains two separate comments on the original matn. The first comment explains why the expression 'musta'bad jiddan' (very far-fetched) is used instead of 'ghayr sahih' (not correct) for the claim that the face is a part subsisting in something. The author distinguishes three meanings of al-qiyam (subsistence): occurrence/inherence (which is impossible for a part), attribution as a descriptor, and subordination in location. The latter two are not properly applicable because the part cannot exist without the whole, so the statement is only 'very far-fetched' rather than outright false. The second comment addresses the phrase 'la khafa' (no concealment) that there is no third thing. An objection is raised: the known face (like 'the walker' in defining man) is known before it becomes an instrument; when we conceive man by 'the walker', we have an observed thing and an instrument, and we seek another instrument for our observed thing. The objection argues that this implies a third thing. The answer is that there is no corruption in one thing being observed from two aspects (as observed and as instrument), so the reality is just this, not a third entity.

Key Terms
القيام
العروض
الحصول
الاختصاص الناعت
التبعية في التحيز
الجزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'musta'bad jiddan' means the claim is completely false.
Correction: It actually indicates only a strong improbability because two of the three possible meanings of qiyam are not straightforwardly disprovable.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say 'musta'bad jiddan' rather than 'ghayr sahih' regarding the part-subsisting claim?
A) Because the part cannot subsist in any sense.
B) Because two of the three possible meanings of qiyam are not applicable, but the incorrectness is hidden.
C) Because the part is not a face.
D) Because the whole can exist without the part.

Key Takeaway
The choice of expression in kalam reflects subtle distinctions in the possibility or hiddenness of falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a nuanced expression like 'musta'bad jiddan', do you stop to consider why the author chose it over a stronger term? How often do you assume that a weaker expression implies doubt rather than careful logical precision? The commentator explains that the part's subsistence is impossible in one sense but only far-fetched in two others. This requires distinguishing between different meanings of the same term. In your own understanding of Islamic beliefs, are there concepts you treat as a single block when they actually have multiple valid interpretations? How can you develop the habit of distinguishing meanings before passing judgment on a truth claim?
?
The passage shows that even a small phrase like 'musta'bad jiddan' carries deep logical reasoning. On the Day of Judgment, you will be questioned about your beliefs and the knowledge you pursued. Have you given sufficient care to understanding the precise arguments of the scholars, or have you accepted summaries without grappling with the fine distinctions? This lesson calls you to a higher standard of intellectual rigor. Resolving the Objection of a Third Thing
Commentator refutes an objection about a third thing by invoking two-aspect observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that a third thing is needed fails, based on the principle that one entity can serve both as observed and as instrument of observation.

Core Idea
One thing can be known from two aspects: as observed and as instrument.

The second comment addresses a potential objection to the statement 'la khafa' (no concealment) that there is no third thing. Someone argues that when we use a known face like 'the walker' as an instrument to define man, we seem to require a third entity. The commentator refutes this by showing that one thing can be simultaneously observed from two different aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the objection: that when we conceive man by 'the walker', we have an observed thing and an instrument, and we need another instrument for the observed, implying a third thing.
2
Identify the rebuttal
The commentator says there is no corruption in one thing being observed by two aspects. So the known face serves both as the observed and as the instrument, not two separate things.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider how in kalam, conceptual analysis can involve one thing having multiple roles. This resolves the need for a third entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (مستبعد جداً إذ القيام بمعنى العروض والحصول به ممتنع في الجزء، وأما بمعنى الاختصاص الناعت، أو التبعية في التحيز، فلأنه لا تتصوّر النعتية والتبعية إلا بعد تعقل وجود كل منهما بدون الآخر، ولا يعقل وجود الكل بدون الجزء، ولأجل الخفاء في عدم صحة المعنيين الآخرين، قال: مستبعد جدا دون غير صحيح. قوله : ( ولا خفاء في أنه ليس هناك أمر ثالث قيل : فيه بحث لأن الوجه المعلوم كالماشي بالنسبة إلى الإنسان كنا نعلمه قبل أن يصير آلة لملاحظة أمر ما هو الإنسان، فإذا تصورنا الإنسان بالماشي ففيه ملحوظ، وآلة ملاحظة حاصلة في هذا الآن، ونطلب شيئاً آخر هو آلة ملاحظة أخرى لملحوظنا، ولا فساد في كون الشيء الواحد ملحوظاً بجهتين، والواقع ليس إلا هذا فليتأمل .
His saying: (Musta'bad jiddan) because 'subsistence' (al-qiyam) in the sense of occurrence and inherence in it is impossible in the part; as for in the sense of attribution (al-ikhtisas) as a descriptor (al-na't) or subordination (al-tabi'iyya) in location, it is because descripthood and subordination are not conceivable except after understanding the existence of each without the other, and it is not intelligible for the whole to exist without the part, and due to the hiddenness in the incorrectness of the other two meanings, he said: musta'bad jiddan (very far-fetched) rather than 'ghayr sahih' (not correct). His saying: (And there is no concealment that there is no third thing) it was said: there is investigation in it, because the known face, like 'the walker' in relation to 'man', we know it before it becomes an instrument for observing something which is man. So when we conceive man by 'the walker', there is in it an observed thing and an instrument of observation present at this moment, and we seek another thing that is another instrument of observation for our observed thing; and there is no corruption in one thing being observed by two aspects, and the reality is nothing other than this, so let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage contains two separate comments on the original matn. The first comment explains why the expression 'musta'bad jiddan' (very far-fetched) is used instead of 'ghayr sahih' (not correct) for the claim that the face is a part subsisting in something. The author distinguishes three meanings of al-qiyam (subsistence): occurrence/inherence (which is impossible for a part), attribution as a descriptor, and subordination in location. The latter two are not properly applicable because the part cannot exist without the whole, so the statement is only 'very far-fetched' rather than outright false. The second comment addresses the phrase 'la khafa' (no concealment) that there is no third thing. An objection is raised: the known face (like 'the walker' in defining man) is known before it becomes an instrument; when we conceive man by 'the walker', we have an observed thing and an instrument, and we seek another instrument for our observed thing. The objection argues that this implies a third thing. The answer is that there is no corruption in one thing being observed from two aspects (as observed and as instrument), so the reality is just this, not a third entity.

Key Terms
آلة الملاحظة
الملحوظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That using a known face as an instrument requires a third entity to serve as the instrument for that face.
Correction: The same entity can serve as both the observed and the instrument from different aspects.

Checkpoint
What resolves the objection of needing a third thing?
A) The known face is not truly an instrument.
B) One thing can be observed from two aspects.
C) The third thing is ignored.
D) The instrument is separate from the observed.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, one entity can have multiple conceptual roles, thus avoiding the need for additional entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on Allah’s attributes, do you tend to multiply distinct realities in your mind instead of seeing one essence with multiple aspects? How can this lesson's principle of dual aspects reshape your understanding of tawḥīd? Commit to pausing whenever you feel the need to separate attributes and remind yourself of this unity.
?
Think of a recent situation where you viewed a person or concept through only one lens, leading to misunderstanding. How does acknowledging that the same thing can be observed from two aspects call you to be more holistic in your judgments? Write down one relationship where you will actively practice seeing from two aspects starting today.
?
Are you guilty of assuming that two different descriptions of a single matter must refer to two separate realities? This lesson teaches that one thing can serve both as an observed reality and as an instrument to know another. Where in your spiritual life have you artificially separated things that should be held together? Resolve to find one such area and treat it with the principle of 'no third thing'.

8 min
No Third Thing in Seeking a Concept – The Two-Aspect Model
The commentator asserts that only two things exist in seeking a concept and replies to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only two aspects (the unknown essence and a known aspect) are necessary when seeking a concept, and how an apparent objection suggesting three things is resolved.

Core Idea
Seeking a concept requires only the unknown essence and a known aspect—no third thing.

In this passage, the commentator refutes the claim that a third thing is needed when we seek to conceive a concept. He explains that any act of seeking a concept involves two elements: the unknown essence we aim to acquire, and a known aspect that allows us to direct our attention. A skeptic might think that when we seek a concept under a specific description, three things are involved, but the commentator shows this is not the case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the commentator's assertive tone and the structure: statement, explanation, objection, reply.
2
Identify the two necessary aspects
The commentator states that when seeking a concept, its essence must be unknown, and some aspect of it must be known. These are the only two things.
The unknown essence is the 'self' of the sought object; the known aspect is some of its attributes.
3
Examine the apparent objection
Consider the example: seeking 'man' under the aspect 'walking' seems to yield three things: the concept, the unknown aspect, and the known aspect. Understand why this seems plausible.
The objection is a common misunderstanding.
4
Follow the commentator's reply
The commentator argues that the 'unknown aspect' is actually the essence itself, so we still have only two: the unknown essence and its known aspects.
This resolves the objection without introducing a third thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزءاً، وإطلاق القيام عليه مستبعد جداً، إلا أن يراد به الحمل ( ولا حاجة ) في دفع هذه الشبهة ) إِليه ) أي إلى إثبات الأمر الثالث لأنها قد اندفعت بما حققناه مع أن إثباته مخالف للواقع، وذلك لأنا إذا أردنا تعريف مفهوم لنتصوره فلا بد أن تكون ذات ذلك المفهوم أي نفسه وعينه مجهولا، وغير حاصل لنا ليمكن تحصيله، وهذا معنى قولنا : المجهول هو الذات أي ذات المطلوب ،وعينه ولا بد هناك أيضاً من أن يكون أمر ما صدق عليه معلوماً ليصح به توجهنا إليه وطلبنا إياه، فهذا هو المراد بقولنا: المعلوم بعض اعتبارات الذات أي بعض اعتبارات ذات المطلوب الذي هو المجهول ولا خفاء في أنه ليس هناك أمر ثالث يتعلق به غرضنا حتى يتصور أن يكون المطلوب أمراً ثالثاً وراء الوجهين فإن قلت : قد يطلب مفهوم الإنسان من حيث هو هو، وقد يطلب وجه من وجوهه، وقد يطلب مفهوم الإنسان بوجه من وجوهه، فعلى هذا التقدير الأخير يثبت أمور ثلاثة مفهوم الإنسان الذي هو المطلوب ووجهه المجهول الذي باعتباره صار مطلوباً ، ووجهه المعلوم الذي به أمكن طلبه قلت : مفهوم الإنسان ذلك الوجه الذي طلب به مفهوم هو المجهول، وهو ذات المطلوب فليس لنا إلا ذات المطلوب المجهول وبعض اعتباراته المعلوم واعلم أن صاحب نقد المحصل أثبت الأمر الثالث إلزاماً للأمام بما ذكره في مسألة المعلوم على الإجمال، حيث قال : المعلوم على سبيل الجملة معلوم من وجه ومجهول من وجه، والوجهان متغايران، والوجه المعلوم لا إِجمال فيه والوجه المجهول غير معلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد ظن أن العلم الجملي نوع يغاير العلم التفصيلي، فإنه قد أن يكون مراده لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد هو الكل من حيث هو كل بل نقول : لا بد من حمل كلامه على ذلك، إذ لو حمل على التغاير بالذات لم يتم التقريب إذ لا يلزم من انتفاء الإجمال في الوجهين انتفاء الإجمال في الشيء ذي الوجهين وحينئذ لا يتم الإلزام إذ ليس في الحقيقة إلا الوجهان، ولا يمكن طلب شيء منهما . قوله: (مستبعد جداً إذ القيام بمعنى العروض والحصول به ممتنع في الجزء، وأما بمعنى الاختصاص الناعت، أو التبعية في التحيز، فلأنه لا تتصوّر النعتية والتبعية إلا بعد تعقل وجود كل منهما بدون الآخر، ولا يعقل وجود الكل بدون الجزء، ولأجل الخفاء في عدم صحة المعنيين الآخرين، قال: مستبعد جدا دون غير صحيح. قوله : ( ولا خفاء في أنه ليس هناك أمر ثالث قيل : فيه بحث لأن الوجه المعلوم كالماشي بالنسبة إلى الإنسان كنا نعلمه قبل أن يصير آلة لملاحظة أمر ما هو الإنسان، فإذا تصورنا الإنسان بالماشي ففيه ملحوظ، وآلة ملاحظة حاصلة في هذا الآن، ونطلب شيئاً آخر هو آلة ملاحظة أخرى لملحوظنا، ولا فساد في كون الشيء الواحد ملحوظاً بجهتين، والواقع ليس إلا هذا فليتأمل . اعترف عنه هناك بأن الشيء المعلوم من وجه والمجهول من وجه، يغاير الوجهين، فألزم ها هنا بأن المطلوب التصوري ليس أحد الوجهين بل الشيء الذي له ذانك الوجهان، ويشهد لما ذكرناه أن هذا المثبت قال في نقل تنزيل الأفكار : المطلوب المجهول هو حقيقة الماهية المعلومة ببعض عوارضها فاكتفى بالوجهين
That it is a part, and applying subsistence to it is very unlikely, unless it is meant by way of inherence. And there is no need in repelling this sophism for it, i.e., to establishing the third thing, because it has already been repelled by what we have verified, while its establishment contradicts reality. That is because if we want to define a concept to conceive it, it is necessary that the essence of that concept, i.e., its self and its identity, be unknown and not obtained by us so that it can be acquired. This is the meaning of our saying: the unknown is the essence, i.e., the essence of the sought object, and its identity. And there must also be something that applies to it known, so that our attention to it and our seeking it may be valid. This is what is meant by our saying: the known is some aspects of the essence, i.e., some aspects of the essence of the sought object which is the unknown. And it is not hidden that there is no third thing that pertains to our purpose such that it might be imagined that the sought object is a third thing beyond the two aspects. For if you say: the concept of man may be sought as such, or a face of its faces may be sought, or the concept of man may be sought by a face of its faces. On this last estimation, three things are established: the concept of man which is the sought object, its unknown face by virtue of which it became sought, and its known face by which its seeking was possible. I say: the concept of man is that face by which the sought concept is the unknown, and it is the essence of the sought object. So we have only the unknown essence of the sought object and some of its known aspects. And know that the author of Naqd al-Muhassal established the third thing as a necessary consequence for the Imam (al-Razi) based on what he mentioned in the question of the known in summary, where he said: the known in general is known in one respect and unknown in another, and the two respects are different. The known respect has no summary, and the unknown respect is absolutely unknown. But when they come together in one thing, he thought that summary knowledge is a type different from detailed knowledge. For he may mean that when they come together in one thing, it is the whole as such. Rather we say: it is necessary to interpret his words that way, because if it were interpreted as difference in essence, the argument would not be complete, since the absence of summary in the two respects does not entail the absence of summary in the thing having the two respects. And then the coercion is not complete, because in reality there are only the two respects, and neither can be sought. His statement: 'very unlikely, for subsistence in the sense of accident and occurrence in it is impossible in the part, and in the sense of attributive specification or following in location, because attribution and following cannot be conceived except after understanding the existence of each without the other, and the existence of the whole without the part is not conceivable. And because of the obscurity in the invalidity of the other two meanings, he said: very unlikely, not invalid. His statement: 'and it is not hidden that there is no third thing' - it was said: there is investigation in this, because the known face, like 'walking' in relation to man, we knew it before it became an instrument for observing something that is man. So when we conceive man through walking, there is an observed thing and an instrument of observation obtained at this moment, and we seek another thing that is another instrument of observation for our observed thing. There is no fault in one thing being observed in two respects, and reality is nothing but this, so let it be considered. He admitted there that the thing known in one respect and unknown in another is different from the two respects, so he coerced here that the conceptual sought object is not one of the two respects but rather the thing that has those two respects. And what supports what we said is that this establisher said in the transmission of Tanzil al-Afkar: the unknown sought object is the reality of the essence known through some of its accidents, so he sufficed with the two respects.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a third thing (amr thalith) is needed beyond the known and unknown aspects in seeking a conceptual object (talab tasawwuri). The commentator begins by stating that establishing such a third thing is contrary to reality. He explains that when we seek to conceive a concept, its essence must be unknown to us so that we may acquire it, while some aspect of it must be known so that we can direct our attention toward it. Thus, there are only two things: the unknown essence and its known aspects. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: one might think that when we seek a concept like 'man' under a certain aspect (e.g., as walking), three things arise—the concept itself, its unknown aspect, and its known aspect. The reply is that the unknown aspect is the essence itself, so we still have only the essence and its known aspects. Next, the commentator discusses the position of the author of Naqd al-Muhassal, who argued for a third thing as a necessary consequence of al-Imam al-Razi's statement about summary knowledge (al-ilm al-ijmali). The commentator argues that al-Razi's words must be interpreted as referring to the whole as a whole, not as a distinct third thing. Finally, the commentator provides a gloss explaining why 'subsistence' (al-qiyam) in the part is impossible, and addresses another objection about the known face serving as an instrument for observation, concluding that no third thing is needed.

Key Terms

المطلوب التصوري
المجهول
المعلوم
الوجه
ذات المطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that when a concept is sought under a specific aspect, three distinct things are involved: the concept, the unknown aspect, and the known aspect.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the unknown aspect is the essence itself, so there are only two things: the unknown essence and its known aspects.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what are the only two things involved in seeking a conceptual object?
A) The known and the unknown aspects, which are the same thing.
B) The unknown essence and its known aspects.
C) The third thing and the two aspects.
D) The concept and its definition.

Key Takeaway
In seeking a concept, only two things exist: the unknown essence and a known aspect that directs our attention to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one deep truth about yourself or your Lord that you have been seeking but feel is unknown? What known aspect—something you already understand—are you using to guide that search? Have you been expecting a third, magical solution instead of using that known aspect?
?
Think of a concept in your faith (like 'taqwa' or 'ikhlas') that you want to understand better. What known aspect of it do you already possess? How can you actively use that aspect today to move from unknown to known, rather than waiting for a sudden flash of insight?

The Naqd al-Muhassal Argument and Summary Knowledge
The commentator refutes the Naqd al-Muhassal's coercion for a third thing by reinterpreting summary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author of Naqd al-Muhassal attempted to force a third thing from al-Razi's statement on summary knowledge, and how the commentator refutes this by reinterpreting al-Razi's words.

Core Idea
Summary knowledge does not imply a third thing; it refers to the whole as such, not a separate entity.

In this continuation, the commentator tackles a more sophisticated argument from the author of Naqd al-Muhassal, who claimed that a third thing follows necessarily from Imam al-Razi's view on summary knowledge (al-ilm al-ijmali). The commentator argues that al-Razi's statement should be interpreted as referring to the whole as a whole, not as a distinct third thing. He also addresses a subsidiary objection about the known face serving as an instrument for observation, and concludes with support from another work (Tanzil al-Afkar).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the commentator's reference to Naqd al-Muhassal and the citation of al-Razi.
2
Understand the coercion argument
Naqd al-Muhassal argues that al-Razi's admission about something being known in one respect and unknown in another forces the existence of a third thing beyond the two respects.
3
Follow the refutation
The commentator insists that al-Razi meant the whole as a whole, not a separate entity. If interpreted as difference in essence, the argument fails.
He also points out that even if there were two respects, they cannot be sought individually.
4
Examine the gloss and subsidiary objection
The commentator addresses the meanings of 'subsistence' (qiyam) and an objection about the known face being an instrument for observation. He argues that even then, no third thing is needed.
5
See the supporting quotation
The commentator cites Tanzil al-Afkar to show that even the proponent of the third thing only mentions two respects in that work.
This reinforces the conclusion that only two things exist.

That it is a part, and applying subsistence to it is very unlikely, unless it is meant by way of inherence. And there is no need in repelling this sophism for it, i.e., to establishing the third thing, because it has already been repelled by what we have verified, while its establishment contradicts reality. This is the meaning of our saying: the unknown is the essence, i.e., the essence of the sought object, and its identity. And there must also be something that applies to it known, so that our attention to it and our seeking it may be valid. This is what is meant by our saying: the known is some aspects of the essence, i.e., some aspects of the essence of the sought object which is the unknown. And it is not hidden that there is no third thing that pertains to our purpose such that it might be imagined that the sought object is a third thing beyond the two aspects. For if you say: the concept of man may be sought as such, or a face of its faces may be sought, or the concept of man may be sought by a face of its faces. On this last estimation, three things are established: the concept of man which is the sought object, its unknown face by virtue of which it became sought, and its known face by which its seeking was possible. I say: the concept of man is that face by which the sought concept is the unknown, and it is the essence of the sought object. So we have only the unknown essence of the sought object and some of its known aspects. And know that the author of Naqd al-Muhassal established the third thing as a necessary consequence for the Imam (al-Razi) based on what he mentioned in the question of the known in summary, where he said: the known in general is known in one respect and unknown in another, and the two respects are different. The known respect has no summary, and the unknown respect is absolutely unknown. But when they come together in one thing, he thought that summary knowledge is a type different from detailed knowledge. For he may mean that when they come together in one thing, it is the whole as such. Rather we say: it is necessary to interpret his words that way, because if it were interpreted as difference in essence, the argument would not be complete, since the absence of summary in the two respects does not entail the absence of summary in the thing having the two respects. And then the coercion is not complete, because in reality there are only the two respects, and neither can be sought. His statement: 'very unlikely, for subsistence in the sense of accident and occurrence in it is impossible in the part, and in the sense of attributive specification or following in location, because attribution and following cannot be conceived except after understanding the existence of each without the other, and the existence of the whole without the part is not conceivable. And because of the obscurity in the invalidity of the other two meanings, he said: very unlikely, not invalid. His statement: 'and it is not hidden that there is no third thing' - it was said: there is investigation in this, because the known face, like 'walking' in relation to man, we knew it before it became an instrument for observing something that is man. So when we conceive man through walking, there is an observed thing and an instrument of observation obtained at this moment, and we seek another thing that is another instrument of observation for our observed thing. There is no fault in one thing being observed in two respects, and reality is nothing but this, so let it be considered. He admitted there that the thing known in one respect and unknown in another is different from the two respects, so he coerced here that the conceptual sought object is not one of the two respects but rather the thing that has those two respects. And what supports what we said is that this establisher said in the transmission of Tanzil al-Afkar: the unknown sought object is the reality of the essence known through some of its accidents, so he sufficed with the two respects.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a third thing (amr thalith) is needed beyond the known and unknown aspects in seeking a conceptual object (talab tasawwuri). The commentator begins by stating that establishing such a third thing is contrary to reality. He explains that when we seek to conceive a concept, its essence must be unknown to us so that we may acquire it, while some aspect of it must be known so that we can direct our attention toward it. Thus, there are only two things: the unknown essence and its known aspects. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: one might think that when we seek a concept like 'man' under a certain aspect (e.g., as walking), three things arise—the concept itself, its unknown aspect, and its known aspect. The reply is that the unknown aspect is the essence itself, so we still have only the essence and its known aspects. Next, the commentator discusses the position of the author of Naqd al-Muhassal, who argued for a third thing as a necessary consequence of al-Imam al-Razi's statement about summary knowledge (al-ilm al-ijmali). The commentator argues that al-Razi's words must be interpreted as referring to the whole as a whole, not as a distinct third thing. Finally, the commentator provides a gloss explaining why 'subsistence' (al-qiyam) in the part is impossible, and addresses another objection about the known face serving as an instrument for observation, concluding that no third thing is needed.

العلم الجملي
العلم التفصيلي
الوجه
القيم
المطلوب التصوري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that summary knowledge (al-ilm al-ijmali) is a distinct type of knowledge different from detailed knowledge, implying a third thing beyond the known and unknown aspects.
Correction: The commentator explains that summary knowledge refers to the whole as such, not a separate entity, so no third thing is required.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how should al-Razi's statement about summary knowledge be interpreted?
A) As implying a third thing distinct from the two respects.
B) As referring to the whole as a whole, not a separate entity.
C) As proving that the two respects are actually the same.
D) As showing that detailed knowledge is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The claim of a third thing from summary knowledge is based on a misinterpretation; the whole as such is not a third entity beyond the known and unknown aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to pin down a 'third thing' in your understanding of Allah's attributes – something beyond His mercy and justice – instead of seeing them as two faces of the same perfect whole? What would it cost you to let go of that extra layer?
?
When you read the commentator's warning not to overcomplicate, what is one belief or practice in your own faith where you have multiplied entities unnecessarily? Will you simplify it tonight, or let it be?
?
The known and unknown faces of a thing both belong to a single reality. Where in your life are you struggling to accept that what you know and what you don't know about a trial are part of the same divine plan? Can you trust the whole without needing to see the part you don't understand?

The Sophism as a Divided Syllogism
Al-Maraghi reduces the sophism to a divided syllogism with two categorical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sophism of seeking a concept is reduced to a divided syllogism and what its components are.

Core Idea
The sophism is a divided syllogism with two categorical premises.

This lesson presents al-Maraghi's analysis that the sophism can be formulated as a divided syllogism (qiyas muqassam). It consists of a disjunctive premise and two categoricals. Understanding this formulation is essential before examining its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the structure of the syllogism: disjunctive, categoricals, conclusion.
2
Identify the components
Identify the disjunctive premise (either perceived or not perceived), the two categorical premises, and the conclusion.
The conclusion follows only if both categoricals are true.
3
Reflect on the form
Consider why this is called a 'divided' syllogism (qiyas muqassam). The categoricals divide the parts of the disjunctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض المتأخرين) هو المولى شرف الدين المراغي إن هذه الشبهة إذا ردت إلى قوانين الاستدلال كانت قياساً مقسماً من منفصلة ذات جزءين، وهو من حمليتين هكذا المطلوب التصوري، إما مشعور به وإما غير مشعور به وكل مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وکل غیر مشعور به يمتنع ،طلبه فالمطلوب التصوري يمتنع طلبه، ولا شك أن هذا الانتاج إنما يصح إذا صدقت الحمليتان لكن قولنا : ( كل مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وكل غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه لا يجتمعان على الصدق، إذ العكس المستوي لعكس نقيض كل منهما ( ينافي الآخر) فإن الأول ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ما لا يمتنع ،طلبه فهو غير مشعور به وهذا العكس ينعكس بالمستوي إلى قولنا بعض قوله : (كانت قياساً مقسماً ) أي كانت مشتملةً على قياس مقسم، فإن ما ثبت به بطلان التالي قياس مقسم والشبهة في نفسها قياس استثنائي كما عرفت، وفيه بحث لأنه يمكن تقريرها هكذا، لو كان التصور مكتسباً لما امتنع ،طلبه، لكن التالي باطل لأن المطلوب لا بد أن يكون معلوماً ومجهولاً، ولا شيء من التصور كذلك لأنه إما معلوم مطلقاً أو مجهول مطلقاً . قوله: ( وهذا العكس إلخ) قيل : إن عكس نقيض كل منهما ينافي عكس نقيض الأخرى، فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار العكس المستوي وليس بشيء لأن المستدل لا يعترف بالمنافاة بينهما، فإنه يقول : إن كل ما لا يمتع طلبه فهو غير مشعور به و مشعور به کالمطلوب التصديقي، وقد قوله: (قياساً مقسماً) القياس المقسم على صيغة المفعول قياس اقتراني مركب من منفصلة وحمليات بعدد أجزاء الانفصال والتأليفات بين الحمليات وأجزاء الانفصال متحدة النتيجة، وكأنه إنما يسمى مقسماً لأن الحمليات منقسمة على أجزاء الانفصال. قوله : (إذ العكس المستوي لعكس نقيض إلخ) لا يخفى عليك أن عكس نقيض كل منهما ينافي عكس نقيض الآخر، فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار الانعكاس بالعكس المستوي، وكأنه أراد أن ينبت التنافي تصريح إحدى المقدمتين، واعلم أن لعدم صدق الحمليتين معاً وجهاً آخر غير ما ذكره المصنف، وهو أن عكس نقيض كل واحدة منهما ينتظم مع عين الأولى قياساً منتجاً للمحال فيقال : مثلاً كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو غير مشعور به وكل غير مشعور به يمتنع فينتج كل ما لا يمتنع يمتنع طلبه، وعلى هذا. قوله: (وهذا أخص من نقيض الثاني لأن نقيض الثاني سالبة لا يحتاج إلى وجود الموضوع ، وهذا معدول محتاج إليه . طلبه
And one of the later scholars, namely al-Mawla Sharf al-Din al-Maraghi, said: This sophism, if reduced to the laws of inference, is a divided syllogism (qiyas muqassam) from a disjunctive with two parts, and it consists of two categoricals as follows: The conceptual sought object is either perceived or not perceived; and every perceived object is impossible to seek; and every unperceived object is impossible to seek; therefore, the conceptual sought object is impossible to seek. There is no doubt that this conclusion is only valid if the two categoricals are true. But our statement: 'Every perceived object is impossible to seek' and 'Every unperceived object is impossible to seek' cannot both be true, because the simple conversion of the contrapositive of each contradicts the other. For the first converts by contraposition to: 'Everything that is not impossible to seek is not perceived'. And this converse converts by simple conversion to: 'Some...'. His statement: 'It was a divided syllogism', i.e., it included a divided syllogism, for that by which the falsity of the consequent is established is a divided syllogism, while the sophism itself is an exceptive syllogism as you know. But there is investigation in this, because it can be formulated as: If the concept were acquired, its seeking would not be impossible; but the consequent is false because the sought object must be both known and unknown, and no concept is like that because it is either absolutely known or absolutely unknown. His statement: 'And this conversion etc.' It was said: The contrapositive of each contradicts the contrapositive of the other, so there is no need to consider the simple conversion. But this is nothing, because the arguer does not admit the contradiction between them, for he says: Everything that is not impossible to seek is not perceived and is perceived, like the assented sought object. And he said: 'Divided syllogism' – the divided syllogism, in the passive form, is a conjunctive syllogism composed of a disjunctive and categoricals according to the number of parts of the disjunction, and the compositions between the categoricals and the parts of the disjunction have a unified conclusion. It is called 'divided' because the categoricals are divided over the parts of the disjunction. His statement: 'The simple conversion of the contrapositive etc.' It is not hidden from you that the contrapositive of each contradicts the contrapositive of the other, so there is no need to consider conversion by simple conversion. It is as if he intended to establish the contradiction by affirming one of the two premises. And know that there is another aspect for the lack of truth of both categoricals together besides what the author mentioned: that the contrapositive of each, when combined with the original itself, produces a syllogism that yields absurdity. For example: everything that is not impossible to seek is not perceived, and every unperceived object is impossible to seek; therefore, everything that is not impossible to seek is impossible to seek. And likewise. His statement: 'And this is more specific than the contradictory of the second because the contradictory of the second is a negative that does not require the existence of the subject, while this is a modified proposition that requires it.'
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the analysis of the sophism (shubha) about seeking a concept as a divided syllogism (qiyas muqassam) by al-Mawla Sharf al-Din al-Maraghi. The analysis begins by reducing the sophism to a disjunctive syllogism with two parts: the conceptual sought object is either perceived or not perceived. Two categorical premises are added: every perceived object is impossible to seek, and every unperceived object is impossible to seek, leading to the conclusion that the conceptual sought object is impossible to seek. However, the commentator notes that the two categorical premises cannot both be true, because the simple conversion of the contrapositive of each contradicts the other. He provides details: the contrapositive of 'Every perceived object is impossible to seek' is 'Everything that is not impossible to seek is not perceived', which then converts simply to 'Some things that are not impossible to seek are not perceived' (though the text is cut off). The commentator then provides a gloss explaining that the sophism itself is an exceptive syllogism, while the refutation uses a divided syllogism. Another gloss discusses the possibility of formulating the sophism differently, showing that the consequent is false because the sought object must be both known and unknown, but no concept is like that. The passage also defines 'divided syllogism' as a conjunctive syllogism composed of a disjunctive and categoricals. Finally, the commentator explains that the simple conversion of the contrapositive is unnecessary because the contrapositive themselves contradict; moreover, he presents an alternative argument using contrapositives to produce an absurdity, and clarifies that the resulting proposition is a modified (ma'dul) one, which requires existence of the subject, unlike the simple negative.

Key Terms
القياس المقسم
المنفصلة
الحمليتان
المطلوب التصوري
المشعور به
غير المشعور به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The syllogism is valid and proves that conceptual seeking is impossible.
Correction: The passage only presents the form; validity depends on the truth of the premises, which are questioned.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maraghi, the sophism reduces to what kind of syllogism?
A) Exceptive syllogism
B) Divided syllogism
C) Conjunctive syllogism
D) Hypothetical syllogism

Key Takeaway
The sophism is formulated as a divided syllogism with a disjunctive and two categorical premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If seeking a concept is either seeking what you already perceive (impossible) or seeking what is utterly unknown (impossible), then how have you ever genuinely sought knowledge of Allah? Does this argument make you question the reality of your spiritual pursuit? How will you move forward in seeking Him, knowing this logical challenge?
?
Think of a time you felt you could not learn a certain religious concept because it was 'beyond you'. Was it truly unknown, or did you already have some preliminary perception of it? How does al-Maraghi's analysis reveal the flaw in your earlier assumption, and what will you do differently next time you face a seemingly inaccessible topic?
?
When you reflect on your own yearning to know the unseen (ghayb), do you catch yourself treating it as something 'perceived' or 'not perceived'? Is there any area of your faith where you have abandoned the search, content with the first branch? What would it mean for you to confront the second branch honestly, and what concrete step can you take tonight to seek a concept you currently consider 'not perceived'?

Logic and Technical Terms
advanced

Why the Premises Cannot Both Be True
Al-Maraghi demonstrates the contradiction between the two categorical premises using conversion rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the two categorical premises in the divided syllogism cannot both be true, using contrapositive and simple conversion.

Core Idea
The premises contradict each other through conversion of contrapositives.

This lesson explores al-Maraghi's refutation. He shows that the two categorical premises cannot both be true because the simple conversion of the contrapositive of each contradicts the other. This undermines the apparent validity of the sophism.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the argument about conversion.
2
Understand contrapositive conversion
Review: 'All perceived are impossible to seek' contrapositive: 'All not impossible to seek are not perceived'.
Contrapositive flips and negates both parts.
3
Follow the contradiction
Notice how the contrapositive of the first premise, when simply converted, yields a proposition that conflicts with the second premise.
The simple conversion of the contrapositive of each premise contradicts the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض المتأخرين) هو المولى شرف الدين المراغي إن هذه الشبهة إذا ردت إلى قوانين الاستدلال كانت قياساً مقسماً من منفصلة ذات جزءين، وهو من حمليتين هكذا المطلوب التصوري، إما مشعور به وإما غير مشعور به وكل مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وکل غیر مشعور به يمتنع ،طلبه فالمطلوب التصوري يمتنع طلبه، ولا شك أن هذا الانتاج إنما يصح إذا صدقت الحمليتان لكن قولنا : ( كل مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وكل غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه لا يجتمعان على الصدق، إذ العكس المستوي لعكس نقيض كل منهما ( ينافي الآخر) فإن الأول ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ما لا يمتنع ،طلبه فهو غير مشعور به وهذا العكس ينعكس بالمستوي إلى قولنا بعض قوله : (كانت قياساً مقسماً ) أي كانت مشتملةً على قياس مقسم، فإن ما ثبت به بطلان التالي قياس مقسم والشبهة في نفسها قياس استثنائي كما عرفت، وفيه بحث لأنه يمكن تقريرها هكذا، لو كان التصور مكتسباً لما امتنع ،طلبه، لكن التالي باطل لأن المطلوب لا بد أن يكون معلوماً ومجهولاً، ولا شيء من التصور كذلك لأنه إما معلوم مطلقاً أو مجهول مطلقاً . قوله: ( وهذا العكس إلخ) قيل : إن عكس نقيض كل منهما ينافي عكس نقيض الأخرى، فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار العكس المستوي وليس بشيء لأن المستدل لا يعترف بالمنافاة بينهما، فإنه يقول : إن كل ما لا يمتع طلبه فهو غير مشعور به و مشعور به کالمطلوب التصديقي، وقد قوله: (قياساً مقسماً) القياس المقسم على صيغة المفعول قياس اقتراني مركب من منفصلة وحمليات بعدد أجزاء الانفصال والتأليفات بين الحمليات وأجزاء الانفصال متحدة النتيجة، وكأنه إنما يسمى مقسماً لأن الحمليات منقسمة على أجزاء الانفصال. قوله : (إذ العكس المستوي لعكس نقيض إلخ) لا يخفى عليك أن عكس نقيض كل منهما ينافي عكس نقيض الآخر، فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار الانعكاس بالعكس المستوي، وكأنه أراد أن ينبت التنافي تصريح إحدى المقدمتين، واعلم أن لعدم صدق الحمليتين معاً وجهاً آخر غير ما ذكره المصنف، وهو أن عكس نقيض كل واحدة منهما ينتظم مع عين الأولى قياساً منتجاً للمحال فيقال : مثلاً كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو غير مشعور به وكل غير مشعور به يمتنع فينتج كل ما لا يمتنع يمتنع طلبه، وعلى هذا. قوله: (وهذا أخص من نقيض الثاني لأن نقيض الثاني سالبة لا يحتاج إلى وجود الموضوع ، وهذا معدول محتاج إليه . طلبه

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The sophism attempted to undermine the necessity of seeking God by presenting seemingly sound premises. Have you ever encountered a doubt that felt logical but you couldn't pinpoint the flaw? This lesson shows that hidden contradictions often lie in our own assumptions. What is one belief you hold that, if examined with similar logical rigor, might lead to a contradiction? Will you examine it today, or continue to avoid the discomfort?
?
Al-Maraghi's refutation required precise conversion rules—not just direct contradiction. When you defend your faith against doubts, do you rely on surface-level answers, or do you dig deep into the logical structure? Are you willing to learn the tools of sound reasoning so that your faith is built on solid ground? What step will you take this week to strengthen your intellectual foundations?
?
The modified proposition (المعدولة) presupposes existence of the subject. This subtlety can either uphold or undermine an argument. In your own life, do you assume the existence of certain 'subjects' (e.g., free will, the soul) without critical examination? What if those assumptions are flawed? This lesson calls you to honesty: Are you ready to subject your deepest convictions to rigorous scrutiny, even if it means letting go of comfortable but unsupported beliefs?

Objection and First Answer on Contraposition
Objection and first answer denying contrapositive conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection that two premises cannot be true together and the first answer denying the validity of contrapositive conversion for universal affirmatives.

Core Idea
Logical conversions assumed by the ancients may lack proof.

This passage presents a sophisticated logical objection against a previous argument and the first response, which challenges a fundamental conversion rule. The objection attempts to show that two premises lead to contradictory consequences via logical transformations, but the answer cuts the chain by denying the transformation itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical moves: deriving consequences from premises, then the denial of a conversion rule.
2
Identify the objection's structure
The objection takes two premises and derives a contradiction. Find how each premise is transformed and why the derived statements are said to contradict.
The objection uses contraposition (عكس النقيض) and simple conversion (العكس المستوي).
3
Understand the first answer
The answer denies that the universal affirmative premise can be converted by contraposition into a universal affirmative. Why is this denial effective?
The answer claims that such a conversion has not been proven as a logical rule.
4
Reflect on the significance
How does denying a conversion rule block the objection? Consider that without that conversion, the supposed contradiction does not arise.
This shows the importance of foundational logical rules in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير المشعور به لا يمتنع طلبه وهذا أخص من نقيض الثاني فينافيه وكذا الثاني ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو غير مشعور به وينعكس هذا العكس بالمستوي إلى قولنا بعض المشعور به لا يمتنع طلبه، وهو أخص من نقيض الأول فينافيه أيضاً، وإذا كان لازم كل منهما منافياً للآخر لم يتصور اجتماعهما صدقاً فأجيب بمنع انعكاس الموجبة الكلية كنفسها بعكس النقيض تارة فإن انعكاس الموجبة الكلية بعكس النقيض إلى موجبة كلية كما هو طريقة القدماء، مما لم يقم عليه برهان. قوله: (أخص من نقيض الثاني) لأن نقيضه سالبة جزئية أعني ليس كل ما هو غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وهذه موجبة جزئية معدولة تقتضي وجود الموضوع. قوله: (إلى موجبة كلية معدولة) وأما انعكاسها إلى موجبة كلية سالبة الطرفين كما أثبته شارح المطالع فلا يفيد هاهنا لأن الموجبة السالبة الطرفين في حكم السالبة البسيطة في عدم اقتضاء وجود الموضوع، فحينئذ يجوز أن يقال: يصدق عكس نقيض كل منهما ولازمه بانتفاء الموضوع، فلا ينافي الأصل المقتضي لوجود الموضوع، وكذا ضم عكس نقيض كل منهما مع عين الأخرى لا ينتج لانتفاء إيجاب الصغرى، وبما ذكرنا تبين أن الجواب المذكور تام واندفع ما قيل: أن قولنا كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو غير مشعور به لازم لقوله كل مشعور به يمتنع طلبه بعكس النقيض أو لا، وهذا القدر كاف في امتناع اجتماع المقدمتين على الصدق، لأنه إن أراد أنه بمعنى العدول لازم له فغير مسلم، لأن الشيء إما مشعور به أو غير مشعور به، وكل منهما مما يمتنع طلبه، فليس لما لا يمتنع طلبه فرد حتى يصدق الإيجاب العدولي، وإن أراد بمعنى السلب فمسلم لكن لا يفيده لما عرفت. قوله: (مما لم يقم عليه برهان) أي على زعمهم وإلا فقد أيد الشارح طريقة المتقدمين ودفع عنها الشبهة التي أوردها الكاتبي وها هنا بحث وهو أن المتأخرين استدلوا على أن الموجبة لا تنعكس موجبة على طريق القدماء لعدم اللزوم في بعض المواضع، لكنا نعلم أنها تنعكس إليها بالمعنى العرفي في بعض المواد فإن قولنا كل إنسان حيوان يستلزم قولنا كل ما ليس بحيوان ليس بإنسان أطلق عليه العكس في الاصطلاح أم لا، بل هو عكس اصطلاحي به الشارح في بعض مصنفاته واستدل عليه بصدق التعريف والانعكاس فيما نحن فيه متحقق، فيكفي في إثبات مطلوب المدعي فالحق أن الجواب هو الثاني وأما اعتراض صاحب الكشف عليه وجواب الرازي عنه فليطلب من شرح المطالع.
That which is not perceived (ghayr al-mash‘ur bihi) is such that seeking it (talabuhu) is not impossible (lā yamtani‘u), and this is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the contradictory of the second, so it contradicts it. Likewise the second [premise] converts by contraposition (bi-‘aks al-naqīḍ) to our statement: 'Everything the seeking of which is not impossible is not perceived (fa-huwa ghayru mash‘ūrin bihi).' And this converse converts by simple conversion (bi-l-mustawī) to our statement: 'Some perceived things are such that seeking them is not impossible.' And it is more specific than the contradictory of the first, so it also contradicts it. And when the necessary consequence of each of them contradicts the other, it is inconceivable that they be true together. So it was answered: by denying that the universal affirmative converts as itself by contraposition in one way, for that the universal affirmative converts by contraposition into a universal affirmative, as is the method of the ancients (ṭarīqat al-qudamā‘), has not been established by proof. His statement: '(more specific than the contradictory of the second)' because its contradictory is a particular negative, namely: 'Not everything that is not perceived has its seeking impossible,' and this is a particular affirmative with a negated term (mu‘addala) that requires the existence of the subject. His statement: '(into a universal affirmative with a negated term)' but its conversion into a universal affirmative with both terms negated (sālibat al-ṭarafayn) as established by the author of al-Maṭāli‘ does not benefit here, because the universal affirmative with both terms negated is in the ruling of a simple negative in not implying the existence of the subject. Consequently it is possible to say: the contrapositive of each premise and its necessary consequence are true with the non-existence of the subject, so it does not contradict the original [premises] that require the existence of the subject. Likewise, conjoining the contrapositive of each with the other premise does not produce a conclusion because the minor is not affirmative. And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that the mentioned answer is complete, and what was said—that our statement 'Everything the seeking of which is not impossible is not perceived' is a necessary consequence of our statement 'Everything perceived has its seeking impossible' by contrapositive or not, and this is sufficient to prevent the two premises from being true together—is repelled. For if he means that it is in the sense of a [logical] consequent (bi-ma‘nā al-‘udūl), it is not conceded, because a thing is either perceived or not perceived, and each of them has its seeking impossible, so there is no individual for 'that whose seeking is not impossible' to make the affirmative with negated term true. And if he means in the sense of negation, it is granted but does not benefit him for the reason you have understood. His statement: '(upon which no proof has been established)' i.e., in their claim, otherwise the commentator (al-shāriḥ) has supported the method of the ancients and repelled from it the doubt which al-Kātibī raised. And here there is a discussion: that the later scholars argued that the universal affirmative does not convert as an affirmative according to the method of the ancients due to non-necessity in some cases, but we know it converts to it in the conventional sense in some matters. For our statement 'Every human is an animal' entails our statement 'Every non-animal is non-human,' whether this is called conversion in technical terminology or not; rather it is a technical conversion (al-‘aks al-iṣṭilāḥī) with which the commentator [al-Ījī?] deals in some of his works and argued for it by the truth of the definition, and the conversion here is realized, so it suffices to prove the claim of the plaintiff. So the truth is that the answer is the second. And as for the objection of the author of al-Kashshāf upon it and the response of al-Rāzī to it, let it be sought from Sharḥ al-Maṭāli‘.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to a previous argument (likely about the impossibility of seeking tasawwur) and gives the first answer. The objection attempts to show that two premises cannot be true together because their necessary consequences via conversion lead to contradiction. The first answer denies that the universal affirmative premise converts by contraposition into a universal affirmative as the ancients held, claiming there is no proof for that conversion. The commentary then explains the logical details: the contrapositive of the first premise would be a universal affirmative with negated subject and predicate, which does not imply existence of the subject, so the objection fails. It also addresses a potential counterargument about logical consequence and concludes that the true answer is actually the second one (restriction of subject), as indicated by the commentator's discussion of the method of the ancients and the later scholars.

Key Terms
الموجبة الكلية
عكس النقيض
العكس المستوي
السلبية البسيطة
الموجبة المعدولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that contraposition always preserves the same quantifier for universal affirmatives.
Correction: The passage shows that such conversion (universal affirmative to universal affirmative) is not universally proven; it may depend on the existence of the subject, and the later scholars disputed it.

Checkpoint
Why does the first answer deny the conversion of the universal affirmative by contraposition?
A) Because it would lead to a contradiction in the premises.
B) Because the conversion has not been proven as a universal rule.
C) Because the subject of the premise does not exist.
D) Because the premise is not actually an affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The first answer to the objection is that the key conversion rule (universal affirmative by contraposition) lacks proof, thus invalidating the objection.

?
